LASERS\n\nEXECUTIVE\n\nSUMMARY\n\nA study was initiated in 1995 by NASA, co-sponsored\nby the U.S. Air Force\nCommand,\nto determine the feasibility of removing\nthe bulk of the threatening\norbital\norbit (LEO) by irradiating\nit with a ground-based\nlaser. The laser energy ablates a thin\ndebris particle, causing plasma blowoff. The dynamic reaction from one or more laser\ngee of the orbit and hastens reentry.\n\n(USAF) Space\ndebris in low-Earth\nsurface layer from a\nhits lowers the peri-\n\nThe study was undertaken\nas an initiative of the Advanced\nConcepts Office at NASA Headquarters\n(HQ), and managed by the NASA Marshall Space Flight Center (MSFC). The study team included USAF\nPhillips Laboratory,\nMIT Lincoln Laboratories,\nNASA MSFC, Northeast\nScience and Technology,\nPhotonic\nAssociates,\nand the Sirius Group.\nA wide range of objects in orbit are characterized\nas orbital debris. The size range of greatest interest is 1 to 10 cm. While objects smaller than 1 cm are extremely\nnumerous\nand difficult to detect, shielding\nagainst them is straightforward,\nalthough somewhat\nexpensive.\nObjects larger than about 10 cm are routinely tracked, and their numbers are small enough that operational\nspacecraft\ncan maneuver\nto avoid them.\nThere remain about 150,000 objects between 1 and 10 cm in size. They are problematic\nto track, too\nnumerous\nto avoid, and shielding against them is very difficult or expensive.\nNASA believes that the debris population\nlikely to exist during\nStation (ISS) is high enough that limited protection\nmeasures\nare being\nThese will protect it against objects up to about 2 cm in diameter.\n\nthe life of the International\nSpace\nincorporated\ninto the ISS program.\n\nVarious strategies for irradiating\nthe debris objects were analyzed,\nincluding those that engage\nobjects in several passes over the laser, and those in which immediate\nreentry is caused by irradiation\nduring a single pass. The latter is operationally\nthe simplest: fire at any debris object the sensors show to be\napproaching\nin favorable circumstances,\nwithout regard to whether it has been previously\nirradiated\nor not.\nThe former requires a plan such as our "steady rain" approach to guarantee\nthat the risk to space assets\ndoes not temporarily\nincrease at any orbital altitude.\nThe statistical characteristics\nof the debris population\nare reasonably\nwell known. Five different\nrepresentative\ndebris objects were defined as reference\ntargets to deorbit. The orbital distribution\nof the\ndebris particles was addressed,\nand the velocity change needed was determined\nto be a few hundred\nmeters\nper second--sufficient\nto cause the perigee to drop to 200 km. Achieving\na 200-km perigee reduces a particle\'s expected lifetime in orbit to a few days.\nThe interaction\nof laser beams with these debris objects was characterized,\nand the range of\ncoupling coefficients\nof the resultin8 plasma blowoff determined\nfrom both experiment\nand theory. The\nrequired incident beam intensity ancl cluration at the objects was then determined\nin order to cause the\nvelocity change necessary\nfor reentry within a few orbits. It was determined\nthat the laser has to place\nmany very short pulses on the objects to avoid self-shielding\nof the generated\nplasma at the object. The\nintensity of the irradiation\nwas also determined.\nOnce the requirements\nat the debris objects were understood,\nthe required ground laser characteristics were then defined, considering,\nthe effects of the atmosphere\non the beam. Effects included in the calculations\nwere turbulence,\nabsorption,\nstimulated\nRaman scattering\n(SRS), stimulated thermal Rayleigh\n\nscattering\n(STRS), whole-beam\nthermal blooming,\nand nonlinear refractive\nwas developed\nthat enables selection of the optimum laser for this system.\n\nindex. A graphical\n\ntechnique\n\nA number of options for detection,\nacquisition,\ntracking, and handoff of debris targets to the laser\nwere investigated.\nThese included radar, passive optical, active optical using the laser itself, and combinations of these. In addition, a novel detection technique was analyzed that uses the many communications\nspacecraft\nthat are or will soon be in orbit as "free" illuminators\nto form a bistatic surveillance\nsystem.\nA spectrum of system concepts\nwas developed,\neach of which meets some or all the system goals.\nThese concepts\nspan a range of costs and technology\nchallenges.\nIn addition, a demonstration\nof the capability on actual debris could be mounted using mostly existing assets for about $20 million.\nThe nearest term operational\nsystem would consist of a Nd:glass laser operating at 1.06 mm with a\npulse width of 5 ns operating\nat a rate of 1 to 5 Hz. It would have 3.5-m diameter optics, operate with a\nsodium guide star, and produce 5-kJ pulses. This system would cost about $60 million, and would cause\nthe reentry of essentially\nall debris in the desired size range in 2 years of operation,\nup to an altitude of 800\nkm. This system would be sufficient to protect the ISS as well as all other satellites in LEO below 800 km,\nincluding the planned Iridium and Teledesic\nsystems.\nMore\nobjects up to\nother civilian\nan additional\n\nambitious technology\nsystems were defined that have the ability to remove all such debris\nan altitude of 1,500 km. This would extend protection\nto the Globalstar\nsystem as well as\nand defense assets. This more advanced system would require an additional\n$80 million and\nyear of operation.\n\nA cursory analysis indicated that a system of this type is not inherently\nan antisatellite\nweapon,\nbeing relatively\nvery weak. It would have to illuminate a typical spacecraft\ncontinuously\nfor years to\ndestroy its structure, and months to make major changes in its orbit, though unintentional\ndamage to some\nsensors and other subsystems\nwould be possible.\nDue to the inherently\nnational character of such a system, if serious interest\ncapability,\nit is likely that the Department\nof Defense (DOD) should be the preferred\noperate it for the benefit of all spacecraft,\nbe they commercial,\ncivil, or defense.\n\ndevelops to pursue\nagency to develop\n\nthe\nand\n\nThe study concluded\nthat the capability\nto remove essentially\nall dangerous orbital debris in the targeted size range is not only feasible in the near term, but its costs are modest relative to the likely costs to\nshield, repair, or replace high-value\nspacecraft that could otherwise\nbe lost due to debris impacts for debris\nparticles greater than about l cm in size. Due to the difficulty in detecting debris smaller than about 1 cm,\nand their great numbers,\nthe presence of an ORION system would not obviate the need to shield highvalue, large, long-lived\nspacecraft to resist impacts of debris particles that are about 1 cm in size and\nsmaller.\nThe study concluded\nthat a demonstration\nsystem should be undertaken\ncost, the ability to detect, track, illuminate,\nand perturb the orbit of an existing\n\nto demonstrate,\nat low\nparticle of debris.\n\nThe study also concluded\nthat the bistatic detection technique could form a needed\nthe current space surveillance\nsystems, particularly\nin the Southern\nHemisphere.\n\naugmentation\n\nto\n\nPROJECT\nIvan\n\nBekey,\n\nSenior\n\nJohn Rather,\nJonathan\n\nAdvanced\n\nConcepts\n\nTEAM\nNASA/HQ\n\nOffice\n\nNASA/HQ\n\nStudy Advisor\n\nW. Reilly\n\nDavid\n\nSpencer\n\nRamaswamy\nCharles\nGlenn\n\n1.\n\nExecutive,\n\nORION\n\nR. Taylor\nZeiders\n\nPhotonic\nMIT Lincoln\nNortheast\n\nScience\nUSAF\n\nSridharan\n\nResearch\n\nAssociates\nLaboratories\n\nand Technology\n\nPhillips\n\nMIT Lincoln\nWestern\n\nInc.\n\nOregon\n\nLaboratory\nLaboratories\nState College\n\nThe Sirius Group\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nProject ORION was undertaken\nas an initiative of the Advanced\nConcepts Office at NASA Headquarters,\nand managed by NASA MSFC. The study team included USAF Phillips Laboratory,\nMIT\nLincoln Laboratories,\nNASA MSFC, Northeast\nScience and Technology,\nPhotonic Associates,\nand the\nSirius Group.\nThe orbital debris population\nhas increased\nat a linear rate since the exploration\nof space began.\nMost of the mass of the debris in orbit is in the form of large objects:\ninactive payloads\nand rocket bodies.\nMost of the risk to space assets, however,\ncomes from smaller objects. The small objects are missionrelated debris, such as bolts that separate in the deployment\nof payloads and, most importantly,\nfragments\nresulting\nfrom degradation,\nexplosions,\nand collisions\nin space.\nIf enough large objects are placed in orbit, the growth in the debris population\nwill change from\nlinear to exponential.\nThis is a result of the collisions\nbetween large and small objects. The population\nmay\nalready have reached the threshold for exponential\ngrowth in certain altitude ranges. Some mitigation\nmeasures\nhave, therefore, been put into place and others are being discussed.\nOne mitigation\nmeasure already being used is spacecraft\nshielding. This technology\nreduces the\nrisk of catastrophic\ndamage, and the production\nof more fragments\nin orbit, in collisions\nwith debris up to\nabout 1 cm in diameter. For the ISS this protection\nwill be extended\nup to about 2 cm for critical areas.\nThere is no technology\npresently available at a reasonable\ncost to shield against debris greater than about\n2 cm and traveling at 10 km/s mean relative speed. This is because the shielding weight penalty is an\nexponentially\nincreasing function of the maximum\nsize of the debris.\nThe additional shielding required just to extend the ISS protection\nenvelope from 1-cm debris particles to 2 cm weighs about 10,000 lb. For a launch cost of $10,000 per lb, the cost simply to launch this\nshielding\nis on the order of $100 million. Development,\nfabrication,\nand integration\ncould double the cost.\n3\n\nAvoidancemaneuvers reanother\na\nmeasure\nalreadybeingusedto dealwith orbitaldebris.Theseare\neffectivefor avoidingobjectslargerthanabout10cmin diameter. bjectsthissizeor largercanbetracked\nO\nreliablyandtheirorbitspredictedwell enough allow thedebristo beavoided.This methodonly applies\nto\nto assets\nthataremaneuverable,\nandis relativelyexpensive thatit requires\nin\nadditionalpropellant.\nPresently the,drawing,board,,are otherconcepts\non\na few\nthatmayeventuallybeuseful.These\nincludea maneuverablecatchers mitt unattachedpayload thespace\nfor\nstation. evicessuchastheseare\nD\ninherentlyexpensive\nandmay notbe ableto respondquicklyenoughto preventcollisions.\nNeithershielding(dueto theweightpenalty)normaneuversbecause thedifficulty of tracking\n(\nof\nandgenerating\nreliableorbit elements) resufficientto mitigatedebrisin the2- to 10-cmregime.Approxia\nmately150,0001-to 10-cmdebrisparticles\narecurrentlyestimated beorbitingtheEarth.Themajority\nto\nof this debrisis foundfrom 200to 1,500km in altitude.The maximumof thedistributionasa Iunctionof\naltitudeis foundaround1,000km. This peakis thoughtto be dueprimarily to a singleevent,the leakage\nof metalcoolantfrom thedamaged\nreactorof a Russian\nsatellite.The remainder f areconsidering this study,we will not becompletelyvaporizingthedebris\nwe\nin\nparticles,nor will they befragmented a largenumber smallerbits.Instead, e havefounda means\ninto\nof\nw\nof deorbitingthe debrisin the 1- to 10-cmrange,he rangethatis expensive shieldagainst nddifficult\nt\nto\na\nto trackreliably.It will still benecessaryo studymitigationoptions(suchasmorepowerfullasersyst\ntems)to address longer-termbut lower-riskproblemof largerdebris.\nthe\nIt is alsorecognized\nthatlarge,long-livedspacecraft uchastheISS will need some shielding even\ns\nif an ORION system is deployed.\nThis is because the flux of debris particles smaller than 1 cm is relatively\nlarge, and the small particles are nearly impossible\nto detect with present technology.\nCollisions can result\nin extensive\ndamage to unshielded\nspacecraft.\nThe overall objective of the study was to determine\nthe technical feasibility,\nthe cost, and the devel,_ment\ntime for using ground-based\nlasers and sensors to remove 1- to 10-cm sized debris from LEO.\nis was further divided into the following specific subobjectives:\nA. Protect\n\nthe ISS and other assets\n\nB. Protect\n\nall Earth-orbiting\n\nassets\n\nin LEO to an 800-km\nto a 1,500-km\n\naltitude\n\naltitude.\n\nWe will show that ORION systems that accomplish\nthese objectives\nmay cost less than the amount\nneeded just to shield the ISS from debris between 1 and 2 cm in size, and wouldhave\nthe potential to protect not just the space station but all other assets in LEO below about 1,500 km.\n\nprovide\n\nThis report is in the form of a summary\nfollowed\na deeper technical\ndiscussion of our analyses.\n\nby seven\n\ntechnical\n\nappendices.\n\nThe appendices\n\nSections 2, 3, and 4, which follow this introduction,\ndevelop three sets of physical constraints\non\nthe ORION system. Section 2 is concerned\nwith the debris properties:\ntheir sizes, compositions,\nand distribution in space, and their optical and radar properties.\nThe interaction\nof solid targets with intense laser\n4\n\nbeamsis considered section3. Section4 is concerned\nin\nwith thepropagation\nof\n\nan intense laser beam\nthrough the atmosphere.\nIn section 5, we synthesize\nthe physical and programmatic\nconstraints\ninto a set\nof requirements\nfor a system. In sections 6and 7, we discuss existing technology\nas it relates to the system requirements.\nSection 6 deals with high-energy\nlasers and related technology,\nwhile section 7 is concerned with sensors and tracking. Section 8 contains our feasible options along with cost estimates.\nIn\nsection 9, we distinguish\nthe ORION concept from anti-satellite\nweapons. Section 10 summarizes\nthe\nstudy, and section 11 presents our conclusions.\nWe follow this with our recommendations\nin section 12.\nAppendix\nA was prepared by Dr. James P. Reilly of Northeast\nScience and Technology.\nIt is a\nthorough analysis of solid-state\nlaser technology\nas it applies to ORION. In particular,\nit addresses\nissues\nof allowable\npulse duration versus extracted energy density, and the cooling requirements\nof repetitively\npulsed solid-state\nlasers as functions of pulse energy. The cooling requirements\ntake into account both\nbeam quality reduction and fracture. Appendix\nB, also by Dr. Reilly, is a unified evaluation\nand side-byside comparison\nof all debris-object\nacquisition\nschemes. These analyses all used a common analysis\napproach,\ncurrent state-of-the-art\nfocal plane and optical telescope technology\ncapabilities,\nand current\nstate-of-the-art\nmicrowave\ndetectors and transmitter\ntechnologies.\nCommon success criteria are applied to\nall detection techniques.\nAppendix\nC, prepared by R. Sridharan\nof MIT Lincoln\noptical tracking systems for ORION. It contains a complete\ndiscussion\nof\nthe laser-target\ninteraction.\nIn addition, it deals with the critical effects of nonlinear processes\nin the atmosphere on pulsed laser beam propagation.\nThese effects include SRS, STRS, and nonlinear refraction\nand\nself-focusing\n(n2). Appendix\nC also deals with the relationship\nbetween laser-produced\nimpulse and\nreduction\nof debris orbital lifetime, laser and systems design, system demonstration,\nand first-order\ncost\nmodels.\nAppendix\nE was contributed\nby Glenn Zeiders of the Sirius Group. Atmospheric\nlinear propagation and adaptive optics are treated thoroughly.\nAlso in appendix D are discussions\nof lifetime of debris\norbit and engagement\ngeometries\nthat reduce the lifetime. Optical system design, including a coelostat\ndesign for the laser installation,\nis included.\nAppendix\nF, by William Dent of Dent International\nin high-power\nlasers. Raup of MIT Lincoln Laboratories.\n\n2.\n\nTHE\n\nDEBRIS\n\nInc., compares\nthe options\nlaser technology.\nsatellites\n\nis treated\n\nin appendix\n\nin\n\navailable\n\nG. It\n\nPARTICLE\n\nOne set of constraints\non the design of both the laser and the sensor systems is the range of characteristics of the debris particles. The microwave\nreflectance\nsets the size and power needed if a radar\nfacility is to acquire and track objects. Similarly,\nthe optical reflectance\ndetermines\nthe size of an optical\ntracking system. The optical reflectance\nalso plays a role in the laser system design, since laser reflection\nfrom a target decreases the momentum\ntransfer. The ablation and ionization properties\nof the particle surfaces also set requirements\non the size, pulse duration, and power of the laser.\nThe roughly 150,000 particles in the size range from 1 to 10 cm, which are the object of this study,\ncan be classified into five distinct groups. Our approach was to examine each category in order to establish\nminimum\nrequirements\nfor the sensor and laser systems. The requirements\nfor the categories\ncan then be\ncompared\nand the requirements\nassembled\nfor a system that deals with all five categories.\n\n2.1\n\nDebris\n\nDistribution\n\nin the I- to 10-cm\n\nSize Regime\n\nA great deal of work has already been accomplished\nin characterizing\nthe debris cloud surrounding\nthe Earth. The Haystack\nradar system of MIT Lincoln Laboratories\nhas done pivotal work in this regard.\nThe work is described\nmore fully in appendix\nC and is illustrated below.\nA sample of the Haystack debris measurements\nis shown in figure 1. The top part of the figure\nshows the number of particles detected per hour in bins of 50-km altitude each. It shows that relatively few\nparticles are detected below 500 km, and that the number of detections\nper hour rises to a level of about\n0.1 per hour per 50-km altitude bin between 500 and 1,500 km. The flux of detectable\nobjects is defined\nas the ratio of the rate of passage of detectable\nobjects to the cross-sectional\narea through which they pass.\nThe flux must be calculated\nfrom the detection rate in each altitude bin, taking h _ geometry into account.\nThe derived flux is shown in the lower part of figure 1. Orbital\n\ndebris\n\nparticle\n\ncharacteristics\n\nmatrix.\n\nMost of the estimated\n150,000 debris particles in the I- to 10-cm size range are in orbits at inclinations ranging from 30 to 99. This has implications\nfor the laser site selection.\nThe latitude requirements\nare somewhat\nrelaxed. The use of Haystack\nitself, in remote association\nwith a laser site at a clear weather,\nclear sky location (such as Albuquerque\nor China Lake) becomes\nan intriguing possibility.\nOnly the Na/K spheres (about 50,000 particles)\nare in nearly circular orbits. The remainder\nof the\ndebris particles\ntravel in elliptical orbits ranging from 1,500-km apogee to 520-km perigee. For example,\nthe bulk of the carbon phenolic fragments are in highly elliptical orbits with apogees around 1,190 km and\nperigees around 610 km. Since the inclination\nof these orbits is about 87 \xc2\xb0, they constitute\na risk to all\nspace-based\nassets in this range; and, since the main source of debris in orbits from 200 to 500 km is\nmaterial entering this range from above, they are a risk to practically\nall assets with orbits below about\n1,200 km.\nThe multispectral reflectivity\nof the debris particles has been investigated.\nThe requirements\npresented to the sensor and laser systems hold no major surprises.\nThe microwave\nreflectivity\nof about 0.1 is\nmanageable\nto more than a 2,000-km slant range by current, proven radar technology\nsuch as Haystack.\nReflection\nat 1.06 microns to more than a 2,000-km slant range is expected to be sufficient to enable fine\ntracking using a laser radar. Reflection\nin visible light is expected to be more than sufficient to allow sunlight tracking at appropriate\ntimes during the day to more than a 2,000-km slant range. A 2,000-km range\nin these categories\nis the maximum\nneeded to track debris at 45 \xc2\xb0 in elevation and 1,500 km in altitude.\nA final conclusion\nfrom figure 3 bears on the laser system requirements.\ncumulative\nDv required to deorbit particles from the five categories\non a single\nrange from 90 to190 m/s. Read up to the curves and find that TA/m a 1 cm 2 da_/t\nNext, as a worst case, look up the lowest\nA/m in figure 4, which is 0.15 cm 2/g for a steel part. 1\n13 divide this into TA/m and find that the\nexpected life in orbit is about 7 days. The product\n\nof lifetime\n\nand area-to-mass\n\nratio as a function\n\nof perigee\n\naltitude.\n\n9\n\nThe increase lifetimewith increasing\nin\naltitudeis oneingredientn a recioefor modelin_thetime\ni\nevolutionof thedebrispopulation.It is truethata particlein theiowerpart of the" 00-500 attitude\n2\nkm\nrangeexits in a shorttime dueto drag.It takesa muchlongertimefor anyoneparticleto moveinto the\nupperpartof therangefrom above. his is offsetby thegreater umber f particles\nT\nn\no\natthetop part of the\nrange.Also, it is the more hazardous particles, with low area-to-mass ratios, that traverse the altitude range\nmost.slowly.\nIn the 18 years it takes the particle of the previous example to move through the 200- to 500_n aJtituae range, many more space operations\ntake place, with thepredictable\nresult that the debris population _ows\nlinearly or exponentially\nm time: One finding of the NRC Committee\non Space Debris t "is that\neven wire current mitigation\nmeasures,\nthe orbital debris population in LEO will continue to grow at a linear rate (if not an exponential\nrate) until well into the next century. Only after many years of both current\nand new mitigation\nmeasures\ncould the population\nbegin to fall.\nHigh laser intensity on the surface of the particle is a key requirement\nfor generating\nsufficient\nDv\nfor deorbit. Two basic operational\nstrategies\nare available. The first is called one pass, one deorbit, and the\nsecond is called steady rain. In the former strategy, the particle is detected soon after it rises above the\nhorizon and a sufficient\nnumber of high energy laser pulses are brought to bear on the surface of the particle. Each pulse ablates a thin layer of the su_ace\nand subsequently\nl_onizes it. The reaction causes a s_mall\nchange in me particle\ns orbit. Sufficient\npulses on one pass bring the perigee below 200 km, which is our\naetlnltion\nof a successful\ndeorbit.\nThe second strategy is to engage lower altitude particles before higher altitude ones. The idea is to\nwalk down, from high to low, a train of particles\nwhile actually reducing the risk to space-based\nassets.\nFor example,\n100-km.bands\ncould be established.\nFirst, only particles in the 200- to 300-km range would\nbe allowable\ntargets. A particle would be lowered from the 200- to 300-kin band to below 200 kin. Only\nwhen a par_tlcle is removed from this range would it be permissible\nto engage a particle in the 300- to 400mn tgand. AS a particle from the 300- to 400-km band falls into the lower band, the risk to assets in the\nlower band is no higher than it had been at first, for one particle was removed at the beginning.\nThen, particles\nin both the 200- to 300-km and the 300- to 400-km bands would be eligible to be\nengaged.\nHowever,\nthe prerequisite\nfor engagement\nin the 400- to 500-kin would be a particle lowered\nfrom the 300- to 400-kin bands and the 200- to 300-km bands. This same scheme would be followed\nin\nmoving to higher altitudes. This steady-rain\nstrategy eliminates the possibility of a temporary\nincrease in\nrisk to space assets caused by failure to deorbit a particle in a single pass. Post-engagement\ntracking is\ndesirable\nin this case, to verify that the particles\nhave indeed been moved to lower orbits.\nAs will be discussed\nlater, the Dv\'s required are such that the one-pass, one-deorbit\nstrategy\nshould be workable\nfor the majority of the debris we have categorized.\nThis means that substantive\ntechnical margin is offered by having the steady-rain\noption as a backup operational\napproach.\nMore details on\nthe strategies\nare supplied in appendix C.\n\n3.\n\nTHE\n\nPARTICLE/LASER\n\nINTERACTION\n\nThe previous section dealt mainly with the debris characteristics\nthat set limits on their detection,\nidentification,\nand tracking. This section deals with the characteristics\nof materials thought to be present in\nthe debris when they are exposed to high intensity\nlight. The pulse energy, mirror size, and repetition\nrate\nrequirements\nfor an ORION laser stem from the surface characteristics\nof the debris particles being irradiated and the momentum\ntransfer needed for perigee reduction.\nThe requirements\non pointing are related to\nthe appropriate\ntimes for engagement\nof debris in elliptical orbits.\n\nNational\nResearch\nNational\nAcademy\n\n10\n\nCouncil Committee\nof Sciences,\n1995.\n\non Space\n\nDebris,\n\nOrbital\n\ndebris:\n\na technical\n\nassessment,\n\n3.1\n\nThe Particle\'s\n\nSurface\n\nAblation of a microthin\nlayer of the particle\'s\nsurface is crucial to providing\na significant\nchange in\nmomentum\nto the particle. Ionization and plasma formation\nfurther enhance the momentum\ntransfer. We\nignore the much weaker radiation pressure that exists in the absence of ablation. A substantial\namount of\nwork has been published\nby the fusion community\nover the past decade, pertaining\nto these interactions\nfor\nvarious materials.\nA wealth of detail can be found in appendix D.\nThe coupling coefficient\nCm is the ratio of the momentum\ntransferred\nto the energy delivered.\nThe\nlaser intensity on the target is the ratio of the power in the beam to its cross-sectional\narea, and the coupling\ncoefficient\nis a nonlinear function of intensity for a particular\nmaterial. The peak of the function corresponds to the laser intensity at which the maximum change in the particle\'s\nmomentum\noccurs for the least\namount of energy input.\nFigure 5 illustrates the coupling coefficient\nfor a single material, nylon, irradiated\nby varying intensities of KrF laser radiation.\nIn this experiment,\nthe pulse duration was fixed at 22 ns. At an intensity of\n2.5\xc3\x97108 W/cm 2, the laser energy is most efficiently\ncoupled to the momentum\nchange of the particle.\nReducin,,= the intensit\nby as, much. as 50 percent, only reduces, the. coupling coefficient\nfrom a maximum\nof\n6.5 to about 6 dyne JJ. Even if the vaporized\nmatermal msnot iomzed, there is good momentum\ncoupling by\nsimple evaporation.\nThis illustrates that there is a relatively forgiving\nthreshold intensity requirement\nfor\nthe laser at the particle, since large (50 percent) variations\nin intensity mean only a small change in coupling efficiency.\nWhat OptimumCouplingIntensityMeans\nIE+I\nExperimental\n\'\n_- - _Cmax=6.5\n\n4\n\nt ......\n\n_\n_\n,:_:_:_:::::: II\n............\n....\n_:....... What\n\ni\n\nI\n\nl\n\n,\n\nl\n\n1.00E+9\nLaser Intensity\n\nFigure\n\ni\n\noptimum\n\n,\n\n,\n\ni\n\ni\n\ni\n\n1\n\n1.00E+10\n\n(W/cm 2)\n\ncoupling\n\nintensity\n\nmeans.\n\nThe intensity of a laser pulse of a given energy depends on the pulse duration.\nA shorter pulse of a\ngiven energy has a higher intensity. To put it precisely, the intensity on the target is the fluence divided by\nthe pulse duration, where the fluence is the ratio of the energy reaching the target to its cross-sectional\narea.\n11\n\nFigure5 illustrates\nthecouplingcoefficientfor a singlematerialanda singlepulseduration.The\nintensityneeded peakcouplingefficiencyactuallydepends n boththepulsedurationandthematerial.\nfor\no\nAs the pulse duration decreases, there is less time for energy reaching the target surface to be conducted to\nthe interior, and the intensity for peak efficiency\ndecreases.\nAlso, metals require a somewhat\nsity for maximum\ncoupling than nonmetals\nbecause they are better thermal conductors.\n\nhigher\n\ninten-\n\nRemarkably,\nwe found that a simple relationship\npredicts the fluence required for most efficient\ncoupling for all pulse durations\nand all materials for which there are sufficient data. The relationship\nis\nshown in figure 6. To use this graph, one chooses a pulse duration on the basis of available technology\nor\natmospheric\nfactors, and then reads the most efficient fluence within a factor of 3 or so. For example, for\npulse durations\non the order of 5 to 10 ns, an incident fluence of about 4 to 6 J/cm 2 provides the optimum\nmomentum\ncoupling for the five categories\nof debris. Recall that the coupling coefficient\ndepends only\nweakly on the intensity in the vicinity of the peak, so the fluence requirements\nare quite forgiving.\nShort Pulses Give Optimum Coupling at Lowest Energy\nLaser parameters for optimum momentum coupling\n(48 experiments, UV-IR, all materials)\nIE+4\nBest power-law fit to all data:\n\n_xa\n\n4) : 2.30E4 * x\'(0.446), R\'2 = 0.73,\n1E+4\n\n_xu\n\nrms fit error = factor of 3.2 (Ioglo = 5:0.51 )\n\n._\nT\n\nA\n\nW\n\n5\n\n>S\n\n_\n\n_6c\n\n_ 1E+4 i\ng\n\n1E+4\n"0\n.m\n\ng\n_\n.,_ _\n\nSymbol\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n_"/_\n\nWavelength Flange\nUV (100-499 nm)\n\nIE+4\n....... _\n_iii_\n..:,,_ _i_\n\n.v\n\n_7\n111\n_\n1E+4\n1E-13 1E-12\n\n_\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n1_\n\n,,,,,\n\no\ni\n\n1E-11\n\nVIS (500-1,059 nm)\nShort ,R (1.06-4.2 pro)\n\nI\n\n1E-10\n\niiii\n\n"]\n\ni\n\n1E-9\n\ni\n\niiili\n\ni\n\ni\n\niiiiii\n\n1E-8\n\ni\n\ni\n\n1E-7\n\niiinll\n\nLong IR (10.6 I_n)\ni\n\n1E-6\n\ni\n\niiiiill\n\ni\n\n1E-5\n\ni\n\nIIIIM]\n\ni i\n\niiillll\n\n1E-4\n\n1E-3\n\nat various\n\npulse\n\nI I\n\nIllll\n\n1E-2\n\nPulse Duration (s)\n\nFigure\n\n6. This study pointed out the need for experimental\nstudies of CW photoablation\nof\nmaterials more complex than elemental\nsurfaces. Also, we have found no studies of laser interactions\nwith\nsurfaces having shapes more complex than flat plates.\n\n3.2\n\nWhen\n\nAnd\n\nHow\n\nOften\n\nTo Engage\n\nAs we showed in the previous section, short laser pulses give efficient momentum\ncoupling at reasonably low fluences. In section 6, we will argue that such fluences are within the capabilities\nof nearfuture technology\npulsed lasers operating from the ground. We will consider issues of laser propagation\nin the next section, but we note here\nthat a fluence of 4.6 J/cm 2 provided by a laser at 1 mm launched\nby a 4.5 m adaptive optic would require\nan energy of at least 3,600 J per pulse at 0 \xc2\xb0 zenith angle, or at least 12,000 J per pulse at 60 \xc2\xb0 zenith angle.\nIf such energies are not available, or not available\nat such a high pulse rate, then it may be necessary to\ndeorbit the steel parts in multiple passes. The other target types will be much easier to deorbit in a single\npass.\n4.\n\nLASER\n\nATMOSPHERIC\n\nPROPAGATION\n\nThis section deals with a third set of physical constraints\non the ORION\nlaser and sensor systems.\nFirst, the relationship\nbetween diffraction-limited\nmirror size and spot size on the particle will be discussed. Next, we consider the intensity and beam quality losses associated\nwith operating through the\natmosphere.\nThese losses can be severe unless properly handled in the design of the laser system. The\nphysical mechanisms\nconsidered\nare atmospheric\nabsorption,\nturbulence,\nand nonlinear\neffects.\n\n4.1 Linear\n\nPropagation\n\nAs we showed in the previous section, a sufficiently\nhigh laser beam intensity on the particle surface is needed to impart the desired momentum\nchange. For a given amount of energy in a pulse of a given\nduration, the intensity is inversely proportional\nto the cross-sectional\narea of the beam at range. We now\nconsider the lower limit on the beam diameter\nin the regime of linear propagation.\nThe spot size is fundamentally\nlimited by diffraction.\nThe diffraction-limited\ndiameter of the spot is\nproportional\nto the wavelength\nand inversely\nproportional\nto the diameter\nof the telescope used to focus it.\nThe smallest spot size is obtained, in principle,\nby using the shortest wavelength\nand the largest mirror\ndiameter available.\nThe largest mirrors in existence\nare 10 m in diameter, but for a moment let us consider a much less\nexpensive\n3.5-m mirror as an illustration.\nAlso, let us take 0.5 mm, which is in the visible part of the spectrum, as a typical "short" wavelength.\nAt the longest slant range of interest, 2,000 km, the spot diameter is\nabout 70 cm. Recall that a fluence of about 5 J/cm 2 is required for most efficient\ncoupling with a 10-ns\npulse. With these numbers,\nwe arrive at a pulse energy of 20 kJ. Pulse energies\nconsiderably\nhigher than\nthis have been obtained with existing lasers. Thus, a simple calculation\nshows that existing technology,\nin\nprinciple, can easily provide the intensity needed for momentum\ntransfer to the most distant pieces of\ndebris under consideration.\nWhile smaller spot sizes further relax the laser power requirement,\nthe fine tracking challenge\ngrows, as does the size of the mirror. For primary mirrors larger than about 3.5 m, aperture size becomes\na primary driver to the cost of the laser system. Designing\nto shorter wavelengths\nreduces the aperture size\nrequirement\nproportionally,\nbut raises serious issues relating both to turbulence\nand the surface accuracy of\nthe mirror.\n\n4.2\n\nTurbulence\n\nand Atmospheric\n\nAbsorption\n\nThe air through which the laser beam passes before leaving the atmosphere\nis not a uniform\nmedium. The index of refraction is a function of the air density. The lower layer of the atmosphere,\ntroposphere,\nis characterized\nby turbulent\nmotion of cells of air with varying density. As convection\n14\n\nor\ncells\n\nmovethroughthebeam,or thebeammovesthroughcells,thebeamtendsto spread\nandlosecoherence\nbecause thedensityvariations.\nof\nFortheORIONproject,it is importantto maintainthebeam\nquality in orderto placesufficient\nintensityontheparticleatrange. his places\nT\ntherequirement adaptive\nfor\nwavefrontcorrectiononthe\nbeamdirectordesign.AppendixE treatstheseissues greatdetail,andwesummarize\nin\nthemhere.\nThe effectsof turbulence thebeamcanbenullified by distortingtheopticsof thebeamdirecting\non\ntelescope a controlledway.This is "adaptiveoptics."The sizeof theindependently\nin\ncontrolledzones\non\nthe correcting\noptic (assumedobeequalin sizeto theaperture) houldbeon theorderof theFriedscale\nt\ns\nro. The Fried\n\nscale\n\nis on the order\n\nof 10 cm for a wavelength\n\nlength. From this we can see that one thousand\nments will be needed to correct a 3.5-m mirror\nAdaptive optics with over\ntems are now under development,\n\nof 1 mm. It decreases\n\nor more independently\ncontrolled,\nin a 1-ram laser beam director.\n\nwith decreasing\nprimary\n\n100 segments\nare already in use for astronomical\nimaging.\nincluding\na system for the 3.5-m STARFIRE\ntelescope.\n\nmirror\n\nwaveseg-\n\nLarger\n\nsys-\n\nThe information\non atmospheric\nconditions needed to correct the mirror cannot come from the\ndebris itself. The light travel time is such that the laser must be pointed up to 100 m ahead of the particle.\nAn artificial beacon, or guide star, must be used instead. A guide star is made with a laser much lower in\npower than the "pusher"\nlaser. The beacon will be aimed ahead of the particle and used to sample the\ncolumn of air through which the pusher laser must pass.\nThe guide star is not effective unless some of its energy is scattered back to the ground to return the\nphase information\nnecessary to distort the correcting\noptics. The beacon laser\'s wavelength\ncan be chosen\nso that some of its energy is scattered back to the telescope from a distinct layer high in the atmosphere.\nAstronomical\nsystems in use today typically make use of the presence of sodium in a layer about 90 km\nabove the ground. It is fortuitous that sodium can be found in this layer, for it is not difficult to build a\nlaser that can excite the sodium atoms into resonance fluorescence\nand return a usable signal to the ground.\nAt the position of the intended laser spot in the sky, the area over which the beam can be corrected\nby a guide star is known as the "coverage\nsize." The coverage size decreases\nas wavelength\ndecreases.\nIf\ndiffraction\nalone were considered,\none would use the shortest wavelength\navailable.\nBut once the coverage\nsize is smaller than the intended beam spot size, it is no longer possible to use the guide star to correct\ncompletely\nfor atmospheric\nturbulence,\nand the beam would spread and fall in intensity. One way around\nthis would be to use more than one guide star. Several closely spaced guide stars could provide the phase\ninformation\nneeded to correct the optics. While this is possible in principle, it has not been demonstrated.\nWe have found that for adaptive primary mirrors 3.5 m in diameter and smaller, a single sodium\nguide star is sufficient to provide the necessary\ncorrections\nfor a wavelength\nof 1.06 mm. If a shorter\nwavelength\nwere used, then a minimum of four closely spaced guide stars would be needed to provide\nsufficient\ninformation\nto make the necessary\nwavefront\ncorrections\nfor a mirror this size.\nA full analysis of the tradeoffs in laser wavelength\nmust take atmospheric\ntransmission\ninto\naccount. The atmosphere\nis highly absorptive\nfor most wavelengths\nof the electromagnetic\nspectrum.\nFortunately,\ntransparent\nand partially transparent\nwindows exist in which the laser beam will propagate\nwithout serious attenuation.\nThe visible and near infrared from 0.4 to 1.3 mm is one window, as is the\ninfrared band from 9.5 to 12 mm.\nAlthough the technology\nexists for powerful\nsmall spot on a target is prohibitively\nlarge. There is\nvisible to near infrared, and it is within this window\nFurther discussion\nof existing laser technology\nmay\n\nlasers at 10 ram, the mirror size required to produce a\nwell-developed\ntechnology\nfor powerful\nlasers in the\nthat the most reasonable\noptions are to be found.\nbe found in section 6.\n\n15\n\n4.3\n\nAtmospheric\n\nNonlinear\n\nEffects\n\nEven though the laser wavelength\nis chosen in a window of atmospheric\ntransparency,\none must\nconsider the possibility\nof beam spreading\nand energy loss by nonlinear mechanisms.\nThese are mechanisms that grow in importance\nas the intensity of the beam in the atmosphere\nincreases, or as the path\nlength in the atmosphere\nincreases. We have made an extensive\nstudy of these effects, including nonlinear\nrefractive\nindex, STRS, SRS, and whole-beam\nthermal blooming.\nNonlinear\nrefractive\nindex tends to degrade beam quality by spreading the beam, since the refractive index tends to increase at high intensity. STRS attenuates\nthe beam by breaking it up and scattering\nit\nin different directions.\nSRS attenuates the beam by scattering it in different directions\nat different wavelengths. Whole-beam\nthermal blooming spreads the beam as it heats the air through which it passes. The\nnonlinear mechanisms\nare depicted in figure 8. Our modeling\nof these effects is treated completely\nin\nappendix D. The limits imposed by the nonlinear mechanisms\non the ORION laser are graphed in figure 9.\nThe beam is assumed to be propagating\nvertically through the atmosphere,\nso that the near-field\nintensity\non the vertical axis refers to the beam as it leaves the laser. The beam is also assumed to originate at sea\nlevel. The graph would appear somewhat\naltered at angles other than vertical, and if the laser were located\nat a high altitude above sea level. The laser pulse duration is shown on the horizontal\naxis. Pulses shorter than this or lower\nthe nonlinear\nmechanisms.\n\nOne possible exception to this "comer of opportunity" view will be considered\nlater for the attainment of subobjective\nB. When the SRS intensity limit first begins to rise for short pulses, it rises so\nslowly that the higher allowed intensity is too little to compensate\nfor the decrease in fluence due to the\nshorter pulse. But, recall that the intensity needed for most efficient momentum\ncoupling\ndecreases\nwith\ndecreasing\npulse length. There is a possible operating\npoint near 100 ps pulse duration where the SRS\nlimit has risen enough to make such operation attractive,\nand where the nonlinear index effect is not yet the\nlimiting consideration.\nIt is important to note how the situation of figure 9 changes when a different wavelength\nis used.\nAs the wavelength\ndecreases,\nthe near-field intensity\nlimits also decrease for a given pulse length. This\nimplies that the smaller apertures permitted by diffraction\nfor smaller wavelengths\ncan only be realized up\nto a point. Beyond that point, smaller apertures are forbidden\nby near-field\nintensities\nbeyond those\nallowed by nonlinear atmospheric\neffects.\n\n5.\n\nLASER\n\nAND\n\nSENSOR\n\nSYSTEM\n\nREQUIREMENTS\n\nThe particle characteristics,\nthe laser/particle\ninteraction, and the atmospheric\npropagation\nform a\nset of physical design constraints\nfor ORION. In this section, the requirements\nare folded together into a\ncomplete set of requirements\nfor the laser and sensor systems. Also included are the programmatic\nconsiderations of cost and schedule. The requirements\non the laser system will be compared\nwith existing technology in section 6. In section 7, the sensor requirements\nwill be related to existing technology.\nThe requirements\nfor the laser are summarized\nin the top row of figure 10. The laser system must\noperate in one of the atmospheric\ntransmission\nwindows,\nsuch as the one shown by the dark band from\n0.4 to 1.3 mm. Beam effects due to turbulence\nmust be minimized\nby active correction\nin which the area of\ncoverage is as large as the laser spot at range.\nIn order to place the critical intensity on the particle\nby operating in the region of opportunity\ndefined by short\nbelow the critical near field intensity (e.g., 3 MW/cm 2 for\ncapable of achieving the critical intensity and fluence (e.g.,\ndebris particle at least at 800 km altitude and preferably\nto\n\nat range, nonlinear effects must be minimized\npulse duration (e.g., 10 ms for 1 mm) and\n1 mm). The laser and corrective\noptics must be\n600 to 850 MW/cm 2, 4 to 6 J/cm 2) on the\n1,500 km.\n\nIf we take the number of debris particles to be 150,000,\nappropriate\nfor subobjective\nB, then the\ntime required to remove all the debris is about 0.3 year/min\ntimes the time for each piece of debris. The\ntime for each piece is an average, which must include off-duty time. For example, if the average operating\ntime to remove one piece of debris is 10 rain, then the time to remove all the debris is 3 years. The time to\nacquire suitable targets, and the repetition rate and maintainability\nof the laser, are all constrained\nby this\ntogether with the progammatic\nrequirement\nthat all debris to be cleared in some definite time, such as 5\nyears.\nThe Haystack radar has shown that in a field-of-view\nof 0.05 \xc2\xb0, the rate of detection of debris particles is about 6/h. Of these, only about 1/h is in circumstances\nsuitable for targeting.\nThe rate must be an\norder of magnitude higher, or the laser will be idle most of the time as it waits for a new target to be identified. Therefore, we recognize that the field of regard for the ORION sensor should measure\non the order\nof 0.50. In section 5, these were\nsynthesized\nto form a full set of constraints.\nIn this section, we review existing laser technology\nin the\nlight of the constraints.\nLaser technology\nis reviewed in appendix F. We will see that the requirements\nconverge on a wavelength\nnear 1 mm and either a pulsed solid state laser or a CW gas laser.\n\n6.1\n\nPulsed\n\nSolid-State\n\nLasers\n\nSolid-state\nlasers have the highest pulse energies available at this time. Each of 10 beams of the\nNova laser at Lawrence\nLivermore\nNational Laboratory\n(LLNL) produces\n10 kJ per pulse. The Beamlet\nlaser at LLNL produces\n20 kJ per pulse. Both of these are Nd:glass lasers. Pulse durations\nof about 1 to\n50 ns are typical for Nd:glass lasers. Thus, these lasers operate in the ORION comer of opportunity\nfor\nreasonably\nsized apertures.\nFor example, for a 10-kJ pulse lasting 10 ns, SRS can be avoided (at 1 mm)\nfor apertures larger than about 0.4 m.\nThe fundamental\nwavelength\nof the Nd:glass laser is 1.06 mm, which is in the visible/near\ninfrared\nwindow.\nThe visible wavelength,\n0.53 mm, is derived with high efficiency\nby frequency\ndoubling in a\nKDP crystal. The shorter wavelength\ninitially appears attractive, since a smaller aperture is required to\nproduce a given spot size. The SRS limit is more stringent for the shorter wavelength,\nhowever,\nand the\nbeam correction\nwould require unproven\nmultiple guide star technology.\nFor the near term, then, the 1.06\nmm wavelength\nis favored, with the shorter wavelength\na strong future possibility.\nThe highest power lasers today are designed for low repetition\nrates. Beamlet, for example,\noperates at under 0.02 pulses per second. The difficulty\nwith higher rates is that nonuniform\nheating of the\namplifying\nmedium degrades the optical quality of the beam. Beamlet can be operated continuously\nat its\ndesigned rate because the cooling system minimizes\nnonuniform\nheating as long as its maximum\nrepetition\nrate _s not exceeded.\nIf we are to accomplish\nORION\'s\ntask without proposing\nlasers much more powerful\nthan those in\nexistence,\nwe must increase the repetition\nrate, or else the deorbiting\nof the debris will take far too long.\nWe are aware of two ways to overcome\nthe repetition rate limitation. One is to fire the laser rapidly without\ncooling and to allow the amplifying\nmedium to heat up uniformly\nso that optical quality is not affected.\nThis is called the "\'hot rod" mode. It is modeled in detail in appendix A. It should be possible to fire up to\n1,000 pulses in a short time interval before the laser is cooled for the next round.\nSmaller lasers have proven that higher continuous\nrates are possible. At LLNL, for example, a\nlaser that produces\n100 J per pulse operates at 6 pulses per second, and is being upgraded\nto 12 pulses per\nsecond. Although cooling of the medium results in nonuniformities,\nthe optical quality is actively corrected\nwith a stimulated\nBrillouin scattering\n(SBS) mirror. The design of such a system is treated in appendix F.\nOverall, the Nd:glass laser at 1.06 mm was found to be the laser with the best potential\nplishing the mission. The technology\nis widespread\nand developing\nrapidly because of activity\nresearch.\n\n20\n\nfor accomin fusion\n\n6.2\n\nPulsed\n\nChemical\n\nand\n\nGas\n\nLasers\n\nCO2 gas lasers operate\nin the mid-infrared\n(IR) band, at wavelengths\nof 10.6 and 11.2 mm. In\norder to be competitive\nwith solid-state\nlasers, they must either be made much more powerful\nor a much\nlarger aperture\nmust be used. For example,\nsince the wavelength\nis 10 times that of the Nd:glass\nlaser, a\ntelescope\n10 times larger would\nbe needed\nto produce\nthe same diffraction-limited\nspot size. This would\nmake the telescope\ndiameter\non the order of 40 m. If, instead,\nthe same size telescope\nwere contemplated\nfor both lasers, the power\nof the CO2 laser would\nhave to be 100 times greater\nto produce\nthe same fluence\non the target.\nSRS would\nthen become\na limiting\nfactor.\nEither\nsolution\nwould\nbe very expensive.\nTwo other lasers that operate\nbetween\nthe near- and mid-IF\nregions\nare HF/DF\nchemical\nand the\nCO lasers.\nNeither\nis as well developed\nas Nd:glass\nor CO2 lasers.\nThey suffer,\nto a lesser degree,\nfrom\nthe same limitations\nas the CO2 laser at longer\nwavelengths.\nThe DF laser is The long\npulse duration of 10 ms would place it well below the intensity for most efficient coupling. The wavelength of 1.3 mm is not far from the Nd:glass wavelength\nand just inside the near infrared window.\nThe repetition\nrates of iodine lasers are as high as 1 pulse per second, with higher rates under\ndevelopment.\nIt is interesting\nto note that these lasers can be made to operate in a CW mode. The\nROTOCOIL\nlaser at the USAF Phillips Laboratory\nis an iodine laser at 1.3 mm with a continuous\npower\nof 40 kW. Although\nthis is somewhat\ndifferent from the pulsed mode of operation we had envisioned\nfor\nORION, our preliminary\nstudy indicates that it could accomplish\nits objectives.\nIn a CW mode, the intensity must be kept well below the peak intensity of a pulsed laser in\navoid thermal blooming\nand STRS. Even so, the average power must be greater than for the pulsed\nsince short pulses couple most efficiently\nat lower fluences. These two conditions\ncan be met, since\nCW laser is constantly\nin use, where the duty cycle of the pulsed laser can be 10 -6 or less. We will\nan iodine CW option in one of our systems to consider for subobjective\nB.\nData on all the lasers considered\n\n6.4 Relevant\n\nElectro-Optical\n\nare included\n\nin figure\n\norder to\nlaser,\nthe\ninclude\n\n11.\n\nTechnology\n\nThe laser system consists primarily\nof a beam director, a guide star subsystem\nfor optical correction, a coarse track handoff system, a fine track subsystem,\nand a high-energy,\npulsed laser. Large aperture systems with corrective\noptics were identified as part of this study. Many of the details are reserved\nfor appendix\nE.\n\nemploys\ndetection\nmission.\n\nThe Advanced\nElectro-Optical\nSystem (AEOS) located in Maui, HI, is shown in figure 12. It\na 3.6-m primary mirror. This facility\'s\nmission is high-accuracy,\nhigh-sensitivity\nsatellite\nand tracking. The STARFIRE\nfacility located near Albuquerque,\nNM, is intended for a similar\nIt employs a 3.5-m aperture for high-sensitivity,\nhigh-accuracy\ntracking.\n\nAEOS and STARFIRE\nuse adaptive optics with a single guide star to correct wavefront\naberrations due to atmospheric\nturbulence.\nThe tracking rates are about 18 \xc2\xb0 per second in azimuth and 5* per\nsecond in elevation. These rates are sufficient to accomplish\nthe ORION objectives.\nAlthough costs will be\nconsidered\nin more detail in section 8, it is interesting\nto note that STARFIRE\ncosts included $7 million for\nthe primary mirror and $10.5 million for the telescope mount. Total facility costs came to about $27\nmillion.\nAnalyses such as this have shown that there are several possible directions\none might take to\naccomplish\nsubobjectives\nA and B. Subobjective\nA could be accomplished\nwith a 1.06-mm laser using a\n3.5-m mirror. Operation\nwould be within the corner of opportunity\nwith 5-ns, 5-kJ pulses. The time\nneeded to do this depends on the tracking and handoff system, which will be discussed\nin the next section.\nIf the sensor system were not capable of 24-h operation,\nthe subobjective\nA could still be accomplished\nby\nusing a somewhat\nlarger telescope\nto obtain a more intense beam.\nRecall that subobjective\nB raises the coverage\nfrom 800 km to 1,500 km. This will require more\nenergy on target at greater range. Three laser systems are promising\nin this regard. One is to develop a\nBeamlet-type\nNd:glass laser for high pulse rates at 10 to 20 kJ per pulse. A 6-m diameter mirror would be\nneeded to avoid SRS. Alternatively,\na 0.1-ns pulsed Nd:glass laser could be developed\nto take advantage\nof the increase in the SRS threshold\nwith decreasing\npulse duration.\nThe third option that seems feasible\nfor this subobjective\nis the CW iodine laser. This would also require a 6-m adaptive optics telescope.\n\n22\n\nAdvanced Electro-Optical System\n3.67 m Telescope Facility\n\nDomeAperture\nDome Roof\n\nActuators\n\nUpper\nDome\nWall\nLower\nDome\nWall\n\nEight\nHydraulic\nSyslems\n\nExp.\nRoom\n\n36.6 m concrete telescope pier\n\n25 cmc.a. secondary:\n3.63 m c.a. Detecting,\nacquiring,\nand coarse tracking a particle the size of a\nmarble at 2,000 km is a challenging\nrequirement.\nHanding off to a laser engagement\nsystem is another\nchallenging\nundertaking.\nSeveral options have been identified that can satisfy the requirements\ndictated by\nthe debris population\ncharacteristics.\nOne proven technology\noption has been the MIT Lincoln Laboratories\nHaystack\nradar. Another is the STARFIRE\npassive optical satellite tracking system in Albuquerque,\nNM.\nThus, there is substantive\ntechnical\nmargin for this aspect of the problem.\nIt is logical to consider active optical tracking, with either a pulsed or CW laser, making\npusher laser in a defocused\nmode. We will see that this has the potential\nto extend the availability\ntracking from the 4 h per day of the passive option to nearly 24 h per day.\n\nuse of the\nof optical\n\nAlso considered\nin this section is the possibility of using existing communications\nsatellite technology to perform debris detection.\nThe forward scattering enhancement,\nmade possible by the location\nsatellites in orbit, makes this scheme possible. The fact that existing systems might be used parasitically\nmakes this option attractive from a cost standpoint.\n\nof\n\nAlong with the foregoing,\nit is important\nto consider the handoff to the laser system and whether\nthe radar can be used remotely.\nFor example, building a clone of Haystack at an optimum laser site would\ncost on the order of $80 to $100 million. Remote radar support during operations\npromises to save the cost\nof building a new radar. With all the requirements\nin mind, we now explore radar, passive optics, bistatic\nradio frequency\n(RF) detection (using communication\nsatellites to illuminate\nthe particles), and laser radar.\n\n7.1\n\nMicrowave\n\nRadar\n\nOption\n\nThe advantages\nof radar include all-weather\noperation and relatively high accuracy in position and\nsignature.\nAs mentioned\npreviously,\nthe Haystack radar developed\nand operated by MIT Lincoln Laboratories at Tyngsboro,\nMA, (42.6* N latitude) is an example of proven radar technology\nwith the potential to\naccomplish\nthe ORION mission. The facility is depicted in figure 13. Haystack\nis probably the most sensitive radar in the world capable of achieving the tracking rates necessary\nfor ORION. Haystack\noperates at\n10 GHz with a range resolution\nof 1 to 10 m and an angular resolution\nof 10 to 50 mrad. Its range rate performance\nis 0.1 to 1 crn/s. Given these performance\nvalues, Haystack\nwould be suitable for use in the\nORION\nsensor mission.\nWith Haystack, the disadvantage of being in a nonoptimum\nenvironment\nfor the laser may be ameliorated by remote handoff. In principle,\nthis is primarily\na software development\nand integration\ntask,\nwhich Lincoln Laboratories\nbelieves can be done with existing technology.\nThe advantage\nof this development would be to utilize the advantages\nof Haystack without having to clone it at the laser facility site (a\nvery expensive\nundertaking).\nThe general importance\nof this development\nis that the handoff technique could be applied to any\nradar. ORION could use any radar that might be available and suitable. One penalty for using Haystack\nremotely would be its latitude, which constrains\nthe minimum\ninclination.\nThis is expected to be only a\nminor disadvantage\nsince it can see the majority of the debris populations.\n\nfield of\ntracking\nthe laser\nrequired\n24\n\nSearch and acquisition\ncould be accomplished\nusing a two-dimensional\nbowtie scan to build up the\nregard. Such a scan pattern at 30 \xc2\xb0 elevation would be virtually leak proof. The radar would provide\nand discrimination\nof the target with a resolution\nless than 200 m. A resolution cell on the order of\nbeam size (about 1 m) is needed for laser engagement\nwith the particle, so the radar would be\nto hand off to a fine track system.\n\nCanonical Microwave Radar Ior ORION\n\nLongRange Imaging Radar\nHigh Power Transmitter (200 kW)\nLarge, PreciseAntenna (120 ft)\n\nLocation\n\nWidebandCapability(1 GHz)\nSensitive Receiver\n\nTyngsboro, MA (42.6" N, 272\xc2\xb0 E)\n\nRadar Frequency 10 GHz\n(S/N on 1 sq. mile target at 1000 km range)\n\nSensitivity\nPulseLength\n\nS/N Ratio\n\n(ms)\n2.0\n5.0\n\n(db)\n61\n65\n\nTrackingPrecision\nRange\n1--10 m\nAngles\n50 _Lrad\nRange rate\n.1-1 cm/s\nFigure\n\n13.\n\nHaystack:\n\ncanonical\n\nmicrowave\n\nradar\n\nfor ORION.\n\nOf course,\nthe engagement\nlaser itself could be used in the fine track role by simply\ndefocusing\nthe\nbeam to capture\nthe particle\nin the 200-m\npixel provided\nby the radar, and then walking\nthe resolution\ncell\nsize down to 1 m. At this point, the laser would engage the particle in order to lower its perigee. In addition to the other advantages\ndiscussed, radar provides knowledge\nafter the engagement\nas to how a particle\'s orbit was affected.\nRemote handoff is extremely promising.\nIt is expected that a reasonably\nstraightforward\ndevelopment will resolve information\ntransfer concerns and minimize\ninformation\ntransfer time lags. Coordinate\nconversions\ncould be accomplished\nby modem computers\nin near-real time either at Haystack or at the\nlaser facility. The USAF Phillips Laboratory\nis presently investigating\npredictive\naccuracy in using such an\napproach.\nA demonstration\nproject would be needed to complete\na detailed solution.\nAlthough\nHaystack is the flagship of operational\nUSAF radar, other existing radars including\nthe\nUSAF HAVE STARE (with significant modification)\nwould be viable candidates\nfor accomplishing\nthe\nORION\nsensor mission, either collocated or in the remote mode.\nMicrowave\nradar approaches\nthe ideal of 24 h per day operation in all weather. It can operate in the\nday or at night, and even in cloudy conditions.\nWe have estimated\nthat Haystack\nor another radar would\nprovide\nup to a 20-h operating window per day, allowing\nfor severe weather and maintenance\ntime.\n\n7.2\n\nPassive\n\nOptics\n\nOption\n\nUsing Sun reflection from the debris particle, high-sensitivity,\nhigh-resolution\npassive optics\noffers the advantage\nof low cost as compared\nto building a new radar. Developing\na remote handoff capability for the radar offers the attractive approach of a complementary\nradar/optics\napproach.\nThis is important, since surveys show that some debris detected in visible light is not detected by radar, and vice versa.\nPassive optics also provide immediate feedback that the particle was successfully\nengaged by the laser,\nsince plasma ignition produces a bright flash in the visible.\nThe disadvantage\nto passive optics alone is that it only operates during times of clear weather when\nthe Sun/particle/observer\nangles are appropriate.\nThis alignment\ntypically provides a 4 h per day window\nfor operations.\nAs will be shown later in discussing\nORION\nsystem options, using passive optics alone\nwill extend the clearing time beyond 2 years for the debris population\nunder 800 km. We have been able to\nkeep it to 3 years by using a larger mirror than for the microwave\nradar option. Another disadvantage\nis\nthat orbit assessment\nwould be difficult with a passive optics system alone. Orbit assessment\nis proven\ntechnology\nexisting at Lincoln Laboratories\nand also used with STARFIRE\nand AMOS.\nFigure 14 shows the STARFIRE\nfacility. A single facility this size located in a suitable location\n(e.g., China Lake) could accomplish\nboth the detection and tracking mission and the particle engagement\nmission.\nIn short, a single $50 million facility such as this could protect ISS and all other assets under 800\nkm.\n\n7.3\n\nThe Bistatic\n\nDetection\n\nOption\n\nOrbital debris is continuously\nbeing illuminated\nby a\nlocated on the ground, in LEO, and in geosynchronous\norbit\nfunctions\nof such satellites, and is available to ORION at no\nnumbers of communications\nsatellites in the next few years,\npotential\nilluminators\nfor use in ORION surveillance.\n\n26\n\nnumber of communications\ntransmitters\n(GEO). This illumination\nis due to the normal\ncost. Dramatic\nincreases are expected in the\nparticularly\nin LEO, and thus in the number of\n\nExampleOpticalSystem\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 STARFIRE, Albuquerque\n,, 3.5 meter F/32 Telescope (Narrow FOV)\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Satellite Tracking\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High Sensitivity\n\xe2\x80\xa2 High Accuracy,\n\nFigure\n\nAngles 10 prad\n\n14. STARFIRE:\n\nexample\n\noptical\n\nsystem.\n\nOne major implementation\noption makes use of the fact that the debris target forward-scatter\ncross\nsection can be much larger than the back-scatter\ncross section usually used for surveillance.\nThis is true\nwhen the target is large compared\nwith the wavelength\nof the radiation, and is nearly in line between the\ntransmitter\nand the receiver (i.e., scatter angle close to 180\xc2\xb0). The most interesting\nsuch application\nof the\nforward-scattering\nenhancement,\nwithout placing any requirements\non the communications\nsatellite, is to\nplace a special antenna array on the ground that would look for radiation scattered or diffracted\nby debris\nfrom any normal satellite downlink\nsignal. The large numbers of satellites (close to 1,000 in LEO in the\ntime frame of the year 2000) would assure that favorable geometries\nfor detection of debris objects occur\nfrequently.\nAnother implementation\noption is to use a ground receiver to detect the radiation scattered forward\nby the debris from the communications\nsatellite\'s\nuplink signal. This differs from conventional\nradar only\nin that the transmitter\nand receiver are not collocated,\nwith the forward scattering\nangle being between 0 \xc2\xb0\nand 180 \xc2\xb0. While with this option the transmitter\nis much more powerful than the typical spacecraft\ntransmitter, most large radars are still more powerful than communications\nuplinks. Nonetheless,\nthese uplinks\nare "free" to ORION, and so will also be considered\nfor debris detection.\n\n27\n\nThe details of our analysis of the bistatic detection option are contained in appendix\nG. Main results\nwere the characterization\nof the detectable\ndebris size as a function of the downlink frequency,\nsatellite\naltitude, and receiving\narmy size and geometry.\nThe detectable debris size decreases\nas the array size\nincreases\nand as the frequency\nincreases.\nFurther improvement\ncan be made if the target is illuminated\nover\nseveral receiver beam widths. The forward-scattering\nenhancement\nis greatest for shorter path lengths, and\nwhen the target is near either the transmitter\nor the receiver. The implications\nare that for a given power\ndensity reaching the ground, the debris is more easily detected with signals from LEO satellites\nthan GEO\nsatellites\xe2\x80\xa2 This is true even taking into account the larger antennas and powers of GEO satellites.\nIn order to model the potential of the bistatic detection, a baseline calculation\nwas performed\nfor a\ndebris particle orbiting at 500-km altitude. The baseline frequency is taken to be 20 GHz, with the satellite\nplacing\n10 -]2 W/m 2 on the ground from an altitude of 800 km. The numbers are consistent\nwith existing\ncommunications\nsatellites. The theoretical\nlimit for a 25-m detector antenna is 0.1 m 2, or 30-cm debris\nparticles.\nThe situation improves\nfor other communications\nsatellite sources. There is an unusually\npowerful\ntransmitter\non the ACTS satellite in GEO. For a target in high-Earth\norbit (HEO), the disadvantage\nof\ngreater altitude is compensated\nby the fact that its effective radiated power is greater, a debris particle\nwould spend a greater time in its beam than for a satellite at a lower altitude, and the forward-scattering\neffect is enhanced\nby the proximity\nof the target to the transmitter.\nThis case is also shown as a curve in\nfigure 15, even though debris at this height was not the subject of the ORION study. With the ACTS\nspacecraft,\ndebris particles of 0.03 m 2, or 17 cm across, would be detectable\nnear GEO with a 25-m\nground receiver array.\n0.1\n\n30 cm\n\nA\n\nE\n\nv\n\nua\n,IC\n\n0.01\n\nACTS Illuminated\n\n10 cm\n\ntlJ\nI-\xc2\xa2.9\nI.M\n\nhe"\nD..\nI.M\n\nI-tu\n\nE\n\no.ool\n\n3 cm\n\nu\nZ\n\nFuture LEO\n\nUplink Illuminated\n0.0001\n\nI\n\nI\n\ni\n\nI\n\n0\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n25\n\nf\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n}\n\n50\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\n\n75\n\ni\n\ni\n\n100\n\nArray Size (m)\n\nFigure\n\n28\n\n15. Performance\n\nprediction\n\nfor bistatic\n\ndetection.\n\n=\n\n,\n\nI\n\n1 cm\n125\n\nPlanned\n\nLEO communications\n\nsatellites\n\nsuch as Iridium\n\nor Teledesic\n\nwill deliver\n\na much\n\ngreater\n\npower density to the ground, principally\nin order to work with portable and other small terminals.\nFrequencies may also increase to 30 GHz. If a piece of orbital debris were to pass between such a satellite and\nan array on the ground in a favorable alignment\nfor forward-scattering\nenhancement,\nthe threshold detectable area would fall dramatically.\nFor an object passing within 50 km of the transmitter,\nthe threshold\ndetectable\nsize is 0.001 m 2, or 3-cm size, when using a 25-m detector array. Equally dramatic is the theoretical threshold\nfor detection of a target passing through the uplink beam of a powerful ground uplink\ntransmitter\nto a GEO satellite. In this case, the same detector array could detect 0.0004 m e, or 2-cm size\ndebris\n\nparticles\n\nat 500-km\n\naltitude,\n\neven though\n\nthe forward-scattering\n\nenhancement\n\nis absent\n\nin this case.\n\nThe calculations\nof detection performance\ndo not account for some losses in signal processing\nand\nare somewhat\nidealized, and thus practical systems would probably have lower performances.\nWhile practical systems would thus only be able to detect larger debris than indicated above, the use of larger antenna\narrays could compensate\nfor the losses. These larger arrays would be more costly, though they would still\nbe receive-only.\nThe implication\nis that bistatic detection can probably be effective for debris particles of a\nfew centimeters\nor larger. Further study must be done to see if a system concept is possible that would\nextend this performance\ndown to 1-cm size.\nIn summary, bistatic detection\non the ground is very promising\n\nradars\n\n7.4\n\nLaser\n\nRadar\n\nusing communications\nsatellites, radar satellites,\nand needs to be investigated\nfurther.\n\nand other tracking\n\nOption\n\nWith this option, the laser system itself would simply be used to perform both the sensing and the\nparticle engagement\nfunctions. As the altitude range to be searched increases, the laser energy must by\nfocused into smaller and smaller spots in order to give an adequate signal-to-noise\nratio. This requirement\nis in conflict with the need to search wider areas at higher altitudes. The conflict can be addressed\nby\nincreasing\nthe pulse repetition\nfrequency of a pulsed laser, but at some point the round-trip\nlight travel time\nwill become larger than the time between successive\npulses. At this point, the next outgoing pulse threatens to blind the detector to the return from the previous pulse. For altitudes above about 600 km, this\nmight require\n\na detecting\n\ntelescope\n\nseparate\n\nfrom the beam\n\nlaunching\n\ntelescope.\n\nThis laser radar option, while it appears to be feasible, greatly increases the complexity\nof the laser\nsystem. The sensing requirements\ndictated by the characteristics\nof the debris population\nwould drive the\ndesign to a 5- to 10-m mirror with multiple guide stars. Clearly, a remote radar and/or passive optics offers\nmore technological\nand cost margin for the 800-km objective. Choosing the objective of removing all\ndebris below 1,500 km makes the laser radar approach more attractive\nas one would already be forced to\nlarger\n\n7.5\n\napertures\n\nSensor\n\nto keep the engagement\n\nlaser spot size small at the longer\n\nranges.\n\nConclusions\n\nFour sensor approaches have significant\ncapability applicable to the ORION mission, thus substantive technological\nmargin has been found in the sensor technology.\nAs was shown previously,\nthis\nwas found to be true for the laser area as well. The findings are summarized\nin figure 16.\nThe two most promising\nnear-future options are the radar and the passive optics. Both offer _,ood\ntracking capabilities, good to excellent discrimination\ncapabilities, and excellent handover accuracy, t_oth\ncan search wide areas of space to detect 1-cm debris out to 2,000 kin. Both offer some damage assessment. Either option can satisfy ORION mission requirements.\nFor a demonstration,\nit may be advantageous to have both radar and optics operating together hand in hand. As will be shown later, the radar\noption is slightly higher in cost than the passive optics. Nevertheless,\nthe radar can operate in allweather/day/night\nconditions, so the rate of detection and hence the rate of debris removal is higher than\nwith the passive optics.\n29\n\nSensor Conclusions\n\nParameter\n\nRadar\n\nPassive Oplics\n\nBistatic System\n\nLaserRadar\n\nSearch\n\n8owtie\n\nWide FOV\n\nNone\n\nDefocus\norFence\n\n>5cm\n>lOcm\n> 20 cm\n\nlcm\nlcm\nlcm\n\nDetection\n500 km\n1000 km\n1500 km\n\n1 cm\n1 cm\n2 cm\n\nTracking\n\nYes\n\nYes\n\nNo\n\nYes\n\nExcellent\n\nGood\n\nUnknown\n\nExcellent\n\nExcellent\n\nExcellent\n\nNA\n\nExcellent\n\nDamageAssessment\n\nExcellent\n\nPartial\n\nNo\n\nExcellent\n\nUtilization\n\n24 h/day\n\n< 4 h/day\n\n24 h/day\n\n24 h/day\n\nAvailability\n\nExists\n\nBuildable\n\nNew\n\nORION\n\nLow for\nHaystack\n\nLow for\nSTARFIRE\n\nUnknown\n\nORION+\n\nHigh/New\n\nModerate/New\n\nDiscrimination\n\nlcm\nlcm\nlcm\n\nHandoverAccuracy\n\nCost\n\nFigure\n\n16.\n\nSensor\n\nconclusions.\n\nWhile\nthe bistatic\ndetection\nsystem\noffers high potential\nfor reduced\ncosts, the\nwell analyzed.\nThe finding\nthat this approach\nhas the capability\nto detect at least 5-cm\nholds implications\nfor several\napplications,\nincluding\naugmentation\nof the USAF\nspace\ntems, and warrants\nfurther\nstudy. As discussed\npreviously,\nthis is future technology\nrequiring\nsubstantive\ndevelopment.\n\n7.6\n\nHandoff\n\nA smooth\ntransition\nfrom coarse\nto fine tracking\nis vital to ORION.\nThe radar provides\nparticle\nlocation\nand velocity\nto a resolution\ncell about 200 m across\nat 2,000 km. Once the particle\'s\norbital\nparameters\nare determined\nby the radar (about\n10 s after detection),\na laser beam defocused\nto the same\nresolution\nwill then\ncontrolled,\n30\n\nas the radar will be precisely\npointed\nto illuminate\nthe same region\nof space.\nThe debris particle\nbe simultaneously\nilluminated\nby both the radar and the fine track laser. An automatic,\ncomputerstep-by-step\nfocusing\nprocedure\nwill then commence\nin which\nthe beam is incrementally\n\nfocused\n\ndown\n\nduring\n\nto the minimum\n\nthis procedure\n\nattainable\n\nto complement\n\nspot size. Radar\n\n(or passive\n\noptics)\n\ncoverage\n\nwill be continuous\n\nfine tracking.\n\nOnce the laser is pointed at the predicted location of the particle with an uncertainty\ncorresponding\nto the minimum\nspot size of the engagement\nlaser, engagement\noccurs and is repeated as long as the particle remains in the window of opportunity.\nRadar tracking and handoff (i.e., tracking information\nupdates) continue throughout\nthe multiple engagements.\nOnce the particle leaves the window of opportunity,\nthe radar assesses the post-engagement\norbit for bookkeeping\npurposes.\n\n8.\n\nSYSTEM\n\nCOSTS\n\nThe first crucial finding provided by this study is that ground-based\nlasers and sensors are a feasible approach to orbital debris removal. As the study unfolded, it became clear that a number of technical\napproaches\nwere feasible, adding confidence.\nFinally, these technical approaches\nwere found to have reasonable costs as compared\nto other orbital debris mitigation\napproaches.\nThroughout\nthe study, cost was viewed as a key factor in developing\nconfigurations.\nCosts were\nprimarily\ndetermined\nby analogy, supported\nby NASA costing models. As a result, two demonstration\nexperiments\nhave been identified, and five affordable\nsystems may work, pending the results of a demonstration. Hence, we are confident\nthat the ORION mission can be accomplished\nwith substantive\nprogrammatic\n\nORION\n\nmargin.\n\nEither the AEOS or the STARFIRE\ndemonstration.\nThis would consist\n\nfacility could relatively easily be adapted to do an active\nof detecting and tracking a cataloged\nparticle with a perigee\n\nof\n\napproximately\n200 km and then modifying\nits orbit to a measurable\ndegree.
