Detailed\n\ncost breakdown\n\n(continued).\n\nFor a cost on the order of $160 million, orbital debris removal can be demonstrated\nas part of a\nphased program\nand the envelope\nof coverage\nextended\nto 1,500 km. Configurations\nB 1, B2, and B3\nremove all debris below 1,500 km (about 150,000 particles). Costs grow because requirements\ndictate\nlarger primary mirrors (5 to 10 m).\n34\n\nFor example, option B 1 total costs were derived to be $140 to $176 million. The breakdown\nfor\nthis configuration\nincludes a 0.1-ns pulsed Nd:glass\nlaser operating at 2 to 4 kJ and 1 to 5 Hz and costing\n$45.9 to $66.9 million. Also included is a Government-furnished\ntelescope\nwith a 6-m adaptive primary\nmirror costing $57.3 to $60.3 million. A new sodium guide star subsystem\ncosts $7.1 to $10.7 million.\nThe radar subsystem\ncosts $16.9 to $21.9 million. Integration\ncosts are expected to range from $12.2 to\n$15.5 million. This is a summary\nof a more detailed breakdown.\nThe total costs for the other configurations were derived in a similar manner.\nOption B2 would use a 10-ns Nd:glass laser both as a pusher and as a laser radar. The total cost is\nestimated\nto be about the same as for option B 1. For option B3, we have assumed the development\nof an\niodine CW laser operating at 2- to 4-MW average power. Our best estimate of the system cost is in the\nrange $172 to $239 million.\n\n9.\n\nNOT\n\nA WEAPON\n\nORION would make a poor antisatellite\nweapon. Each laser pulse ablates a layer only a few molecules thick. Thus, at the energy levels delivered,\nburning a hole through the skin of a satellite would take\nyears. Deorbiting\na satellite might be accomplished,\nbut it would take months of dedicated\noperation.\nHence, accidentally\nbringing down a satellite is not possible. Satellite sensors looking directly at the laser\nsite may be blinded, and some other spacecraft\ncomponents\ndamaged, but this can easily be avoided with\nthe proper operating procedures\nat the laser site. The procedures\nwould include avoidance\nof illumination\nof known spacecraft,\nwhich is a technique being used today with complete\nsuccess. As a result, the\nORION system could be operated without endangering\nany declared active spacecraft.\n\n10.\n\nSUMMARY\n\nThe orbital debris population\nposes a significant\nrently, millions of dollars are planned toward mitigating\ntion as well as shielding and maneuvers.\n\nthreat to the ISS and other assets in LEO. Curthe risk, which includes curtailing\ndebris produc-\n\nThe characteristics of the orbital debris population\nincluding size, shape, composition,\nreflectivity,\naltitude, and inclination\nare reasonably\nwell known. The laser/particle\ninteraction\nand plasma dynamics\non\nextremely\nshort timescales\nare sufficiently\nunderstood.\nLaser propagation\nthrough the atmosphere\nis constrained by many effects including turbulence,\nabsorption,\nand SRS. Very short pulses allow us to work\nwithin the limits imposed by these physical phenomena.\nSeveral proven ground-based\nlaser and sensor technology\noptions have been found to allow construction\nof feasible systems. Sensor technology\nincludes ground-based\nradar systems (e.g., Haystack)\nand high-sensitivity\npassive optics that will provide the detection and coarse tracking. Laser options\ninclude a repetitively\npulsed Nd:glass laser operating at 1.06 mm with a 3.5-m adaptive optics primary\nmirror and a single sodium beacon. The integration\nof the sensor and laser options were more than suffi_\ncient to remove all debris below 800 km. An advanced system using technology\nbecoming\navailable in the\nnext 5 years will extend this envelope to 1,500 km.\nFor a cost on the order of $20 million, orbital debris removal can be demonstrated.\nFor an additional cost on the order of $60 million, or $80 million total, essentially.all\norbital debris in the 1- to 10-cm\nsize range below 800 km can be eliminated\nover 2 to 3 years of operation,\nthus protecting\nthe ISS and other\nassets (e.g., Iridium, Teledesic)\nagainst debris of these sizes. A cheaper system capable of debris removal\nonly to 500-km altitude could be used if the sole objective were to protect the ISS. For a total cost on the\norder of $160 million and an additional year of operation,\nthis envelope\ncan be extended\nto 1,500 km, thus\nprotecting\nboth ISS and Globalstar.\n35\n\nThebistaticdetectiontechnique\nusingcommunicationsatellites,houghnot selected inclusion\ns\nt\nfor\nin the recommended\nsystem architecture\nat present, may prove to be an inexpensive\nand readily implemented means to augment the nation\'s space surveillance\ncapability.\nIt may be particularly\nuseful to detect\nand catalog debris in the southern hemisphere,\nwhere there is a dearth of sensors at present.\n11.\n\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nfeasible.\n\nRemoving\n1- to 10-cm debris from LEO using ground-based\nlasers and ground-based\nAll five debris categories\ncan be brought down in 2 to 3 years of ORION operations.\n\nsensors\n\nis\n\nThe study objectives\nhave been achieved. Reasonable\nconfidence\nexists that the systems are feasible in the near term. Suitable hardware and facilities exist in the United States to accomplish\na demonstration experiment.\nGiven the high cost of shielding individual\norbiting assets, particularly\nagainst debris\nlarger than 2 cm, it is strongly recommended\nthat a demonstration\nbe initiated immediately\nas an alternative\nor complementary\ndebris mitigation\napproach.\nRussian progress in ORION-related\ntechnological\nareas has been impressive.\nsubstantive\ncapabilities\nand facilities, and are eager to apply these to an international\nbe considered\nin any plan of action.\n\nThey presently enjoy\nproject. This should\n\nDue to the inherently national character of an ORION-type\nsystem, if serious interest develops to\npursue the capability,\nit is likely that the DOD should be the preferred agency to develop and operate it for\nthe benefit of all spacecraft, be they commercial,\ncivil, or defense, with NASA playing a supporting role to\nensure benefits to the ISS. There may be sufficient motivation\nto pursue the bistatic detection surveillance\ntechnique, whether an ORION system is deployed or not.\n\n12.\n\nRECOMMENDATIONS\n\nMaximizing\nthe use of Government-furnished\nequipment\ngram to find, track, and push a suitable particle presently in LEO\nters.\n\nhardware, initiate a demonstration\nproand verify the change in orbital parame-\n\nThis demonstration\nshould focus on using an existing high energy laser. Preferably,\na Nd:glass\nlaser operating at 1.06 mm should be used in conjunction\nwith an existing adaptive mirror such as STARFIRE or AEOS. The remote application\nof Haystack\nshould be demonstrated\nas part of this, as well as the\napplication\nof passive optics.\nA few existing, cataloged\n(i.e., tracked by U.S. Space Command)\ndebris targets with suitable\ncharacteristics\nshould be identified.\nBoth Haystack\nand the passive optical tracker should be demonstrated\nagainst these targets. The laser should then be used to engage the debris, and the resulting change in orbit\nparameters\nshould be measured.\n\noption\n\naugment\n\n36\n\nBased on further study, demonstration\nfindings, and accurate\neither to accomplish\nthe 800- or the 1,500-km mission.\nPerform\ndebris\n\ncost estimates,\n\na definitive\nstudy of bistatic detection as a surveillance\ntechnique\ndetection capability,\nparticularly\nin the Southern Hemisphere.\n\nselect a configuration\n\nand its application\n\nto\n\nTECHNICAL\n\nA.\n\nAdvanced\nORION\nTechnology)\n\nLaser\n\nB.\n\nTarget\n\nfor ORION,\n\nC.\n\nEngagement\n\nD.\n\nAnalysis\n\nE.\n\nORION\n\nF.\n\nSelection of Laser Devices and Neodymium\n(Dent International\nResearch, Inc.)\n\nG.\n\nBistatic Detection of Space Objects Using\nC. Note that in the\nregion attractive to ORION ( 5 to 50 ns ) the allowable output fluence at 1.06 microns is between l0 and\n20 joulesJcm 2 for glass and YAG hosts doped with Nd ions.\nIt is well known\n\nfor both gas lasers and solid-state\n\nlasers, that oscillator\n\nor resonator\n\nextraction\n\ntechniques produce the highest extraction efficiency and the most compact and lighter-weight\nlaser\ndesigns, while master-oscillator/power-amplifier\n(MOPA) extraction techniques\ncan provide higher\nbeam quality, more flexibility\nand tighter control of the output waveform and phase / frequency content\nof the output beam at the price of larger, heavier and more cumbersome\nlaser system designs.\nSINCE MINIMIZING\nCONSIDERATION\n\nFLOOR-SPACE\n\nAND WEIGHT\n\nFOR THE GROUND-BASED\n\nIS NOT AN OVER-RIDING\n\nORION\n\nCONCEPT,\n\nWHILE\n\nMAXIMUM\n\nFLEXIBILITY\nAND CONTROL\nAT HIGH BEAM QUALITY IS OF UTMOST IMPORTANCE,\nHAVE CHOSEN THE MOPA AS OUR RECOMMENDED\nLASER ARCHITECTURE.\nThe next mode\nlaser. Clearly\n\nof operation\n\nfor single-pulse\n\nto be chosen\n\noperation,\n\nis the cooled\n\nno cooling\n\nvs uncooled\n\nis considered.\n\nversion\n\nFor rep-rated\n\nWE\n\nof the solid state\n\noperation\n\nhowever,\n\nwhether to cool or not IS an issue. Clearly for continuous 24 hrs / day operation, we require active\ncooling. However, for an operating mode where one 30 seconddebris\nengagement\noccurs every 10\nminutes or so in one two-hour period at dawn and another at dusk ( a very real possibility for a viable\nnear-term system), one must question whether ACTIVE cooling is necessary during lasing, or just a\nrapid cooldown between shots. These two operating scenarios can result in VERY different laser\ndesigns, with the former (active cooling while lasing) being a MUCH more difficult ( and hence timeconsuming and hence expensive)\nlaser design than a simpler, cheaper and potentially more robust\nsystem which simply needs to be cooled down between bursts. It is the latter system which is discussed\nin this report.\nB) Key Issues\nFigure 4 lists the issues which must be considered in any solid-state laser design as to damage,\nperformance\nas a simple laser energy source, and performance\nas a source of coherent radiation.\nWe assume in this report that issues of damage and performance\nas an energy source are taken care of\nby good engineering\ndesign. For Nd Yag, up to 20 joules/cm 2 are allowed ( ie LIP TO MATERIAL\nDAMAGE\nTHRESHOLDS\n!!! ) before the temperature\ndifferences are noticeable.\nIf we limit the beam to the 1-20\njoules/cm\n\n2 region,\n\nno gross extraction\n\neffects\n\nshould be seen in uncooled\n\namplifiers.\n\nThe major\n\nthermal\n\ndifferences\nwill therefor be dominated by pump uniformity --that is good engineering\nof the pump lamps\nand their optical cavities. Another cut at this conclusion\nis shown in Figure 18, which indicates that\nflat-top (or equivalently\nsuper-gaussian\nshaped) amplifier pulses are not required for radial thermal\nuniformity at the I0 joule/cm 2 output level.\nAs to temperature\n\nlevel, the "Hot Rod"(ref\n\n4) or "Heat Capacity\n\nMode"\n\n(ref 5) or Thermal\n\nInertial Laser" (ref 3) methods of operation .... all equivalent ,simply different names given to the same\nconcept-has very reasonable upper temperature-level\nlimits before the gain begins to decrease. Figure\n19 shows the centerline temperature\nrise after a single pulse as functions of the gain slab thickness and\npump pulse irradiance. Clearly, the more solid gain medium we have (ie the thicker the slab) the less\nthe temperature\nrise produces in the slab by the given energy delivery. The LLNL Beamlet laser and\nothers\n\nat LLNL used as models in this study\n\npump in the region\n\n0.2 to 2 joules\n\nof pump light per square\n\ncm of slab surface area, have been successful cooling this energy density with active gas or liquid flows\nfor truly-continuous\nrepetititve-pulse\noperation of Beamlet laser designs for the National Ignition\nFacility (Ref 7 ). These two models\n\n(\n\nnumber density and flux density distributions)\nare then used to obtain the requirements\non a purely-radar\nsystem\nas well as a purely-optical\nsystem to provide acquisition of all targets of interest ( currently estimated at between\n30,000 to 100,000 in number) in the orbital altitude regions of interest to NASA.\nDefinition of the Target Set\nAs can be seen in Figure\n\nI ( ref.\n\nl&2),\n\nthe 6 canonical\n\nobjects\n\nof interest\n\nto ORION\n\nare varied in size,\n\nshape, material, orbital parameters and optical properties. Since the majority, of the debris population is currently\nbelieved to be Target Class A (the NaK spherical solids), we shall concentrate on the acquisition of these objects,\nknowing that we will have to examine the robustness of the acquisition systems proposed for consideration\nas a\nresult of these analyses. The model is within a factor of about 2 of reproducing\nthe\nHAYSTACK\nresults, and gives us a more general tool to examine detection techniques and strategies.\nAs an example, a general search over the entire 300 to 1500 km altitude band set up on the basis of the\nTOTAL number of objects expected ( nominally 100,000 ) spread uniformly over the band would expect the\nobjects to have a 3D spacing of about 125 km, and a 2D separation of about 50 km. As seen on Figures 4 and 5\nThe beam sizes and scan rates so determined would find it very difficult to search the lower altitude region\n( where the debris density is far lower than the ensemble mean), and would produce much higher detection rates\nthan expected in the high-debris-density\nregion around 1000 km. For example, a narrow beam passive optical\nsystem (ie, one with no range resolution) searching for debris in the 400 - 600 km altitude region would, from\nFigure\n4, want to have a search Field Of Regard with a radius of about 200 km, and it would scan inside that\nradius with a Field Of View set by debris signature Signal-to-Noise\nand Signal-to\noptimized integration times and scan patterns. These will be discussed below.\nGeneral\n\nDiscussion\n\nof Acquisition\n\nBackground\n\nratios with\n\nApproaches\n\nIn general, acquisition of remote objects for observation and tracking is accomplished\nby the observation of\neither self-emitted or reflected optical energy, RF energy, acoustic energy or other quanta in comparison to some\nbackground\nlevel. In particular, only optical and radar sensors are usable to acquire ORLON targets. The three\napproaches\nbelow are ones which currently appear to even have a chance, given the slant ranges, object sizes and\nsensor characteristics involved.\nI.\n\na microwave radar - with characteristics\nsimilar to the MIT/LL HAYSTACK\nbeam (repeated linear one-dimensional\nscan) or a "bow tie" (repeated two-axis\n\n2.\n\na passive optical system - an astronomical-class\ntelescope\nperhaps with an angle-scanning\ncapability along\nthe lines suggested by MIT/LL for a modified HAYSTACK-type\nradar. The illumination\nof the objects would\nbe by sunlight. The size of the instantaneous\nField of View of the system fixes the instantaneous\nspot size\nbeing viewed, while the angle-scanning\ncapability determines\nthe search Field of Regard.\nan active illuminator\nlaser system - economy dictates that if this option is chosen, the transmitter would use\n\n3.\n\nthe Pusher laser as the energy source,\nfor the detection function.\n\nbut would use a de-focused\n\nIn the sections below, we discuss in detail the driving parameters\nexperimental\ndata and demonstrated\ncharacteristics\nof operational\ntrack the ORION target set debris objects.\nSummary\n\nof MIT / LL\'s\n\nAll-Radar\n\nAcquisition\n\nradar, but with a scanned\nscan).\n\nbeam to interrogate\n\na large spot in space\n\nfor each of the above approaches, and using\nhardware, suggest approaches to acquire and\n\nApproach\n\nThe all-radar approach has been extensively analyzed by MIT/LL during the course of the first phase of\nORION. A radar system with beam parameters similar to those existing at the HAYSTACK facility is required\nfor detection, acquisition,\nidentification,\ntrack and handover to the "pusher" laser system. HAYSTACK has been\nused by NASA to detect, acquire, identify and track space debris objects down to approximately\n1 cm diameter\nat orbital altitudes from 300 km to over 1500 km Using HAYSTACK\nin a staring mode at 0 \xc2\xb0 zenith angle ( ie,\n90 \xc2\xb0 elevation angle) with 1 millisecond pulses, a 40 - 50 hz rep rate and a l milliradian FWHM beam\ndivergence, detection rates of up to 6 / hr were recorded on objects larger than 1 cm ( estimated from RCS data).\nThis means that 6 objects l cm in diameter or larger entered the beam with a measurement\narea of\n74\n\nm\n\nID)\nC\nmm\n\xc2\xa2,,)\n\n"_-\xc2\xb0\n\n4)\n"0\nI\n\n.F=\n\nnm\n\nL--\n\na\n\nO\n\nC\n\no\nio\nIO\n\nX\n\nco\nm\n-ocD\n\n"r-\n\n_\n\n_,_"\n\nx\n\n"Iv\n\n>\n\nt\'X\n\nI|\n\nC\n\n||\n\nII\n\n||\n\n_J\nC\n"0\n\n\xc2\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2_-\n\n,.-\n\n0\n\nX\n\ne-\n\nr-\n\nID\n\n009\nO0_t\nOOtt\n\n\'_\n\n006\n\ni\no\n\nOOL\n009\n\nU\n\n_-\n\ni\n\n001_ _\nf OOg I.\n00_1.._\nq\n\n............\n:....................... _............... 0o_;\n_\n.........\n\n4LId\n\n,4ILl\n\nt,t,J\n\n00g\nsJet,_u \'3 \'su!q _Z_11e tu_l OOI. u!\n\' el)_gle ua_q\xc2\xa7 Ze m-_lO\nu_W\xc2\xa2l\nJ_-tu I_/_._._1o\n\nt-\n\nr,,O\n\nO\n\n_.:_ls\n\nIZUeWl (O-Z) pel=_lo_d\n\nuz_u\n\nqqe"umlap \'me.z uoR=_ep\n\n75\n\nO;\nC\n\nX\n\nIm\n0\n\nm\n"0\n\nr\n\n\\\n\n.D\n0\n\n13\n\nm\no\n\n.C\n\nQ\n\n"i\n\n._\n^\n\nc\nm--\n\nc\nm\n\nc\nm\n\n009_\nO09t\nOOC;\n_.\n00_ t\n00_,_\n\n00\xc2\xa3_\nE\n\n00_\n\n_\nOOt_\n\noom =_\n\n0o6\n11116\n11118\n\nOOL\n\nOOL\n\nOO9\n\nOO9\n\nO09\n\n00_\n\nO01,\n00\xc2\xa3\ni\xc2\xa2)\n\xc3\xb7\nUJ\n\noo\xc2\xa3\n9\n\n9\n\n9\n\n9\n\nW\no.\n\nW\n_.\n\nUJ\no.\n\n_\n\n9\n.\n\nLU\nQ\n\na(-m b$/$t._qo elqet,_:l_p\n"top-(] \'ml_ .uo_,qep\n\n76\n\nW\no.\n\nkU\no\n\nsJa_u\nepn_e\nI_\n\n\'$\' $.uFIopn_e uJ\'400_ u!\nue_.8 W s;oe.fqo uee0xsoq\n(0\xc2\xa2) lq_ um.u\n\napproximately\n1 km in diameter (ie, 1 millirad x approximately\n1000 km range) by approximately\n1200 k m high\n(ie, 1500 km max. altitude minus 300 km min. altitude). Since the length of the radar pulse used was 1\nmillisecond,\nthe length of the "slug of light" moving along the radar line-of-sight\n(LOS) was 300 km, hence\nrange resolution is only on the order of 150 km in LOS. At orbital velocities of 7 krr_\'sec (appropriate\nto the 300\nto 1500 km altitude range), the stay time of a debris object in a 1 km wide beam is no longer than 0.14 second, so\none would think that high PRF\'s could be used to yield more data when a debris object entered the beam.\nHowever, since the round-trip time between the transmitter and the debris band was only 2 millisec ( Ibr 300 km\naltitude) to 10 milliseconds (for 1500 km altitudes), PRF\'s less than only 100 hz were allowed without "blinding\n" the received\n\nsignal with the next outgoing\n\npulse.\n\nThe achieved total detection rate of 6 / hr is too low for ORLON, especially considering the fact than\nabout 50% of these will be climbing toward zenith (and hence a candidate for the Pusher laser), but 50% will be\nexpected to be descending from zenith ,and so are not candidates.\nIn fact, at a useful detection\nwould take 33,000 hrs (4 years at 24 hrs/day, 365 days/years of dedicated operation) to detect\n\nrate of 3/hr, it\n100,000 debris\n\nobjects of interest, EVEN ASSUMING THERE WERE NO REPEATED DETECTION\nOF THE SAME\nOBJECT ON SOME SUBSEQUENT\nPASS OVERHEAD.\nClearly, a different approach is required.\nMIT has proposed that another approach to the use of the radar be considered to dramatically\nincrease\nthe detection rate : that of a "picket fence" rather than a stationary staring beam be used, along with a longer\npulse, to increase the measurement\narea from the single-beam\nl km x 100 km to one _ith 10 km (or more) x 100\nkm.\nIn the picket fence ( or as MIT/LL terms it, the "bow tie" mode) the beam would scan the sky at its\ncurrent scan rate so that effectively\n10 or more angular beam positions are used to define a broader area through\nwhich the debris must pass. Sincethe debris orbital velocity is on the order of 7 krrt/sec, and the inertial velocirv\nof a staring radar beam at the debris altitude is only on the order of 0.5 km/sec (inertial velocib\' = earth rotation\nrate x earth-radius-plus-orbit-altitude),\nit is appropriate to scale the measured entrance rate of detectable debris\nfrom the existing HAYSTACK data to the debris entrance rate into a beam "array" linearly with the measurement\narea\n\ninterrogated\n\nby the beam.\n\nThe fence would be erected\n\nnot at zenith, but rather at a low elevation\n\nangle, perhaps\n\nat 30 \xc2\xb0 elevation\n\nor\n\nless, to allow subsequent time for handover to the Pusher laser and for impulse deliver3. prior to debris reaching\nzenith. For example,\nobjects/hour,\n\nthe HAYSTACK\n\nradar, in the mode used for NASA\n\nfor the detection\n\nof 6\n\nused:\n1. a stationary beam at 90 \xc2\xb0 elevation angle\n2. a beam divergence of I milliradians\nFWHM\n3. an interrogation\nrange of from 300 km out to 1600 km\n4. a pulse repetition\n\nFor these conditions,\n\nthe beam was about\n\nrate less than about 50 hz\n1 km in diameter\n\n(FWHM\n\npoints of the intensit3. _profile).\n\nThe orbital\n\nvelocity at 1000 km is about 7 km/sec, and so the stay-time of a potential debris particle is less than 1 km / 7\nkm/sec = 0.14 sec. At 50 hz, the radar can illuminate the object up to 7 times, making for a more precise\ndetermination\nof tracking data, but at the same time slowing up the detection process. Substantially\nhigher PRF\'s,\nhowever, can NOT be used because the round-trip time limits the PRF to less than 100 hz beyond which the\nreturn signal from the debris is "blinded" by the next outgoing radar pulse. This would not be the case if separate\ntransmitter and receiver dishes were used, but these instruments are extremely expensive, and the option is not\nconsidered further in this discussion.\nDividing the surface area of the spherical shell of debris around the Earth at an\narea of the 1 km radar spot gives 8.7 x 10 s such spots. SNR can be made high enough to satis_ most data acquisition and data reduction systems / techniques\nby varying integration time between readouts, requiring the "on-chip-binning"\napproach suggested bv MIT/I.L.\nLooking at the plots shown in these three Figures, we find that detection during full daylight using sun\nillumination is extremely difficult, if not impossible due to the bright day-sky optical background:\n400 km slant range -- full daytime:\n1200 km slant range -- full daytime:\n3000 km slant range -- full daytime:\nwhile searching\nextremely\n\nat Dawn or Dusk with a sun-illuminated\n\nhigh contrast\n\nSBR = 5E-4\nSBR = 5E-5\nSBR = 8E-6\n\ntarget against a dark sky background\n\nproduces\n\nratios ( or SBR\'s) \xe2\x80\xa2\n400 km slant range -- full night-sky\n1200 km slant range -- full night-sky\n3000 km slant range-- full night-sky\n\nSBR = >IE20\nSBR = >1E20\nSBR = >lE20\n\nIn addition, the Figures show the strength that on-chip binning adds to the passive detection technique. Signalto-Noise ratio rises as the square-root of integration time, and with a dark sky background\n(all but eliminating\nsky-generated\nphotons),\ntimes mean more signal\nallows all these photons\nwhen the pixels are read\n\nthe only serious noise sources are read-out noise and shot noise. Longer integration\nphotons into the receiver aperture, and hence more shot noise, but on-chip-binning\nto be collected on an adjustable-size\n"macro-pixel",\nwhich produces less readout noise\nout as one "macro-pixei"\ninstead of as individual units. Although\nsearching is only possible for the total of 4 hours per day at dawn and dusk, the entire altitude range 300 -1600\nkm can be completely searched to acquire the (currently-estimated\nfrom the MIT/LL data) 100,000 or so debris\nobjects 1 cm or larger in diameter in a period of less than nine to twelve months at current slew-rate capabilities.\nThe follwing Figure,\nFigure 14-A shows that to detect all of the objects in any given altitude bin ( an hence all\nthe objects in all bins) in under 1 year requires a fence about 10 km wide at altitude -about .010 radian. At slew\nrates of .030 rad/sec,\n\nacomplete\n\nraster scan would take 0.67 seconds,\n\nan easy task as can be seen in Fig 7.\n\n5.Active\n\nOptical Acquisition using a Repped-Pulse\nLaser Illuminator\nIn the above sections,\nthe validity of the passive optical acquisition system was established using sunlight\nas the illumination and slewed telescope as a collector so as to provide as much detection area as possible,\nleading to a total search time requirement less than 9-12 months. The active-slewing\napproach was necessary,\nsince the debris objects are only visible against a dark-sky background\n(ie at dawn and dusk) for a total of up to 4\nhours per day. The advantage of an active repped-pulse\nlaser illuminator is to provide illumination\non demand,\nnot just at dawn and dusk as with the Passive Optical Acquisition\nsystem described above. Because of the variation of the spot size /\n\npulse energy requirement\nwith slant range, we choose a spot size characteristic\nof each of the slant ranges above.\nSince the laser illuminator spot is truly a quantity which can be varied from pass to pass of target objects, this is a\nvalid exercise\n\nto help choose\n\nthe required\n\npulse energy and hence size the laser device hardware\n\nrequired\n\nTable 5\nCharacteristic\nAlong with Target\n\nSpot Size Requirements\n\nfor SNR > 1\n\nStay Time in Spot ( Spot Diameter\n\n/ Orbital\n\nVelocity)\n\nFigure\n\n15 ---\n\n400 km slant range ---300 meter dia spot --<0.040\n\nsec stay time in spot\n\nFigure\nFigure\n\n16 --17 ---\n\n1200 km slant range --- 120 meter dia spot---<0.019\n3000 km slant range ..... 40 meter dia spot .... <0.007\n\nsec stay time in spot\nsec stay time in spot\n\nInspection of Figures 18, 19 and 20 rapidly shows that signal-to background\nratio (SBR or contrast ration)\nnot the problem we face here .... it is signal-to-noise\nratio SNR. At energy levels below about 300 joules per\n\nis\n\npulse, the SNR is always less than unity, and only is above about 2 at energy levels above about 1000 joules per\npulse, using this small receiver dish (3.75 meters). Our attempt to adjust integration time has no effect, and\nactually HURTS\nthe SNR for pulses longer than about 1 microsecond.\nTable 5 above shows the spot sizes\nrequired for this 1 kilojoule active illuminator (min energy for SNR > 1). In addition ,if we detect a potential\ntarget debris object in the spot, we would like to hit it at least 3 times to build confidence that it is a real object\nand not a spurious signal. Table 5 also shows a rough estimate of the maximum time a debris object will stay in\nthe beam (spot diameter / orbital velocity), in order to estimate the burst-mode capabilib" the illuminator should\nhave, to provide not only repped-pulse\nactive scanning\nbut also a burst of pulses to provide low false-alarm and\nhigh detection probability data that that given by a single return. A burst rep rate capability of 75 hz (i.e., 1 /\n(.040 sec / 3) to 425 hz ( 1 / (0.007 sec / 3) may be required of this i kj or greater laser used as an illuminator.\nWe conclude\norbital altitude\n\nthat initial detection\n\nby active illumination\n\nregion of interest 200-1500\n\nof the small 1 cm diameter\n\nkm, with a 3.75 meter receiver\n\ndebris\n\ndish at an elevation\n\nobjects\n\nin the\n\nangle of 30 degree\n\nis not very effective for small laser pulse energies. At laser illumination energies above 1000 joules, however, the\nconcept does become effective, but with small 40-300 meter diameter spots for the 400 - 3000 km slant ranges\nrequired. It would probably require pulse rep rates in excess of a few hundred hertz. If we now wish to use such\na system (pulse energies above 1 kilojoule,\n3.75 meter collector dish, pulse durations less than 1 microsecond\n),\nthen in order to survey the entire sky and acquire the estimated 100,000 objects in less than 1 year, the picket\nfence approach discussed above for the radar and passive-optics\napproach must be used. At the longer range ( 1500 km altitude, or 3000\nkm slant range), the required laser is i 000 hz ( and at least 1000 joules per pulse !!! ), and because the optical\nround-trip time is now 0.02 sec with an interpuise time of 0.00| sec, a separate dish for the transmitter and\nreceiver is a necessity.\nWe must conclude from this that initials debris detection by an active illuminator appears to be a diMcult task, if\nit is to be used to acquire all the objects within a one-year time frame over the orbital altitude ranges of interest.\n6.Active\n\nOptical\n\nAcquisition\n\nusing a CW Laser\n\nIlluminator\n\nIn the above, the validly of the passive opticai acquisition system was established using sunlight as the\nillumination\nand slewed telescope as a collector so as to provide as much detection area as possible,\nleading to a\ntotal search time requirement\nless than 9-12 months (see Figure 21 ; 20 hrs day operation for i year results in\n7300 hours of search time). The active-slewing\napproach was necessary, since the debris objects are oniy visible\nagainst a dark-sky background\n(ie at dawn and dusk) for a totai of up to 4 hours per day. The advantage of an\nactive CW laser illuminator is to provide illumination on demand aii during the day and night if necessary\', not\njust at dawn and dusk as with the Passive Optical Acquisition system described above. The equations describing\nthe return signal photons are identical to those of the repped-puise\nilluminator, with the exception that the\nillumination\nis continuos,\nnot pulsed, it is, as was the repped pulse illuminator, single-frequency\n(the laser\nxvaveien_h)\nnot broadband (like sunlight), and we have the same direct control on its intensi_ and duration, via\nthe illumination spot size and laser pulse width.\nThe intensity\n\nlevels which can be delivered\n\nto the interrogation\n\nvolume\n\ndepends\n\nof course on the laser\n\npower level, the Strehl ratio for the beam at interrogation\nrange, the laser spot size chosen to do the searching\nthe atmospheric transmission from the transmitter to the range in question. The MINIMUM beam divergence\nangle will be that of a diffraction-limited\nbeam\n\n0mi n =\n\n106\n\n_. / D\n\n, F_-t-IM\n\nangle\n\nfor\n\na\n\ndiffraction-limited\n\nbeam\n\nand\n\nand at 1000km range, the FWHM spot diameter for a 3.5 meter diameter\ntransmitter aperture with a 1.3 micron\n(i.e., a CW Iodine laser) or a 1.06 micron ( CW glass laser, which exist at power levels below 1 kw)\nwavelength\nis about 0.3 meters in diameter. The mean power densities in the FWHM spot are functions of the\nchosen range, spot size ( with 0.3 meters as a lower limit), illuminator power level, and are shown Figure 22\nfor a Strehl ratio of O.5 and an atmospheric\ntransmission of 89% (calculated\nwith the MODTRAN\nII code ( ref\n3) for Iodine, and 80% for the solid-state\nThe Figure illustrates\nsensor quantum\n\nthat\n\nefficiencies\n\nlaser wavelen_h.\n\nin order to produce\n\nirradiances\n\nhigher\n\nthan those of sunlight\n\nby AT LEAST\n\nthe ratio of\n\nat 1 micron vs 0.5 micron:\n\nIhr_ake,.en 0.1 w/cm 2 (sunlight)\n=\n\nx 65%\n\n/20 % = 0.33 w/cm 2 (Laser req\'m\'nt)\n\nwith the minimum spot deliverable at the chosen 1200 km slant range, the laser must be just under 1 kilowatt\nl micron. Such lasers exist in the industrial laser community. However, the spot diameter is only 30 cm in\ndiameter, and so the beam will have to be slewed rapidly to cover the entire sky in the picket fence search\n\nat\n\npattern\n\nin under one year.\nFigures 23 & 24 show the SNR and SBR for a ! micron CW illuminator producing 20, 200 and 2000\nw/cm2 at a 1200 km slant range against a daylight-sky background ( Figure 23 ) and against a moonless-night\nsky background (Figure 24). ! ....\n\n,- \xc2\xb0\'E\n\n___\n\n-\n\ng\n\n_\n\n_!\nlli_\'_\n\n.--\n\n_\n\n,,--\n\nConclusions\n1.\n\nThe scanned\n\nRADAR\n\nbeam concept,\n\nusing a HAYSTACK-like\n\nmicrowave\n\nradar appears\n\nable to\n\naccomplish the detection of the estimated 100,000 debris objects of interest to ORION in less than 1 year\'s time\nof dedicated operation (20 hrs / day. 365 days / year). To accomplish\nthis, the current HAYSTACK detection\ndata on this class of debris objects0ver\nthe orbital altitude region of interest dictates that the beam be scanned\nlaterally in a pattern termed a "bow tie" pattern to minimize leakage through the large planar measurement\narea\napproximately\n1 beamwidth thick, 10 (or more) beamwidths\nwide (azimuthal\nsinusoidal scan ) and the full orbital\nrange high (300 km to 1500 kin), controllably-oriented\nto put the large-area face perpendicular\nto the dominant\ndebris stream being measured. The RADAR would point at about 30 \xc2\xb0 elevation angle from the horizon, to\nprovide sufficient\nto zenith.\n\ntime tbr Pusher Laser irradiation\n\nbefore the\n\ndetected\n\ndebris\n\nhas risen in the sky from horizon\n\n2.\nThe angle-scanned\npassive-receiver\noptical system, using sunlight to illuminate the debris objects at\nboth dawn and dusk for approximately\ntwo hours each period also appears capable of the same success as the\nRADAR system described above by IVllT/LL. This approach would use sunlight as the illuminator source and\ndetect the diffuse scattering againsi a dark-sky background. We expect that a similar (low---say 30 \xc2\xb0) elevation\nangle from the horizon would be used as with the RADAR, for the same reasons. Detection in daylight using\nreflected sunlight appears impossible against the background\nof the bright day sky. The same concept of a\ncontrolled azimuthal scan to produce a "picket fence" measurement\narea ( configured as was the RADAR "bow\ntie") produces a large measurement\narea. However, it is also necessary to used a focal plane array which is\ncapable of "on-chip binning", to allow the collection of reflected signal photons from the debris object to be\ncollected on an addressable sub-array of pixels on the focal plane (with correspondingly-longer\nintegration times)\nand reading this sub-array out as a single "macro-pixel",\nso minimizing electronic read-out noise photoelectrons\nwhile maximizing signal photoelectrons.\nThe angle-scanned\npassive receiver dish (the same 3.75 meter system\nused for the Pusher Laser) appears able to accomplish the detection of the estimated\n100,000 debris objects of\ninterest to ORION in less than 1 year\'s time of dedicated operation ( 4 hrs / day, 365 days year).\n3.\n\nInitial debris detection\n\nby an active rep-pulsed\n\nanalysis, is very difficult. While it offers the opportunity\nilluminate the debris, not the sun), the active illuminator\n\nlaser illuminator,\n\nwhile\n\npossible\n\naccording\n\nto this\n\nof 24 hr / day operation(since\nit uses the laser to\ndetection concept appears to require substantial fluence\n\n(joules/cm 2) on target to achieve Simaal-to-Noise\nRatios greater than unit)\'. We find\nRatio (ie, the contrast between the dav sky and the reflected signal) is not a problem\ntechnique. The high required fluence implies high laser pulse energies and/or small\nsmall spots imply high pulse repetition frequencies to achieve the desired detection\nless than l year, even with the "picket fence" detection scheme.\nTrade-offstudies\n\nthat Signal-to-Background\nat all in this sensing\nillumination\nspots---however\nof the 100,000 object set in\nreported here indicate that\n\nfor very low orbits (the approximately\n200 km Space Shuttle altitude), the laser illuminator\'s\npulse energy and\nrep rate are low ( 300 to 1000 joule per pulse, at 20 hz using 300 meter diameter spots for illuminating,\nand a\npicket fence pattern approximately\n167 beamwidths\nwide ) .Attempting to achieve to same l-year total detection\ntime for objects at 600 km orbital altitudes (1200 km slant range), the laser energy could be the same, but the\nillumination\nspot has to decrease in size to provide more reflected photons which, over the longer path to the\nreceiver, would give the same SNR as for the lower altitude detection. Calculated\nrequirements\nfor the 600 km\n111\n\naltituderegionarefor a 300\ndiameter\n\n- 1000 joule laser pulse at 150 hz, using a 120 meter diameter illumination spot\nat the slant range of 1200 km and a picket fence pattern approximately\n420 beamwidths\nwide. The\n\nrequirements\n\nfor an illuminator\n\nuseful at the 1500 km altitude\n\n(3000 km slant range)\n\nare much more demanding.\n\n4.\nInitial debris detection by an active CW laser illuminator,\nwhile not extensively studied in this\nanalysis, appears certainly do-able. It offers the opportunity of 24 hr/day operation at moderate transmitter\npower levels. It\'s function is to simply act as a brighter sun in the measurement\nfence" scanned beam and same on-chip binning concepts as the sun-illuminated\n\narea, using the same "picket\ndetection scheme, and while\n\nsuffers a bit due to the lower quantum efficiency of detectors at 1 micron as compared to those in the visible (0.40.7 micron ), merely has to overcome sky back_ound.\nIt offers an additional plus--the transmitter might also act\nas a crude laser ranger, to assess the performance\nof the Pusher Laser after impulse deliver-,.\n\n112\n\nAPPENDIX\n\nENGAGEMENT\n\nC\n\nSTRATEGIES\n\nRamaswamy\nMIT, Lincoln\n\nAND\n\nRISK\n\nSridharan,\nLaboratories\n\n113\n\n2. ENGAGEMENT\n\nSTRATEGIES\n\nAND\n\nRISK\n\n2.1. The Problem\nThe mission\n\nof the ORION system\n\nis to reduce significantly\n\nthe risk to manned assets in space\n\nand, to a lesser extent, provide a cleaner environment\nin space for satellite operations.\nExtensive work by\nNASA/JSC\nhas resulted in a model of debris density and flux in space parameterized\nin various ways.\nChapter\n\n1 of this report contains a review\n\ndebris from space using\nThe obvious\n\nof these models.\n\nChapter\n\n2 addresses\n\nstrategies\n\nfor removing\n\nORION.\n\nstrategy for removal\n\nof debris is to reduce the perigee height\n\nto below\n\n200 Km. in a\n\nsingle period of irradiation. This might be called the "deluge" strategy. An alternative strategy could be to\nreduce the perigee height in steps of say 200 Km. This might be called "steady rain" strategy. In particular,\nthe latter strategy might be forced on the ORION system if an adequately powerful laser cannot be found.\nThis chapter will analyze the risk entailed\nstrategies to be tested/followed.\n2.2. Debris\n\nin either strategy\n\nand make recommendations\n\nfor one or more\n\nFlux and Risk to Spacecraft\n\nThe most extensive\n\nset of data on debris of sizes > 8 mm. has been collected\n\nby the Haystack\n\nradar during the last four years. These data have been extensively analyzed by NASA/JSC 1. There are\nseveral ways the potential risk to space assets can be derived and represented\nfrom these data. BINS)\nFig. 2.2 : Flux through Haystack\n2.2. The Deluge\n\nBeam\n\nStrategy\n\nGiven an ORLON laser of sufficient power, this strategy would require the system to track and\nirradiate any debris piece that is detected. Per Glenn Zeiders\' calculations,\nit is adequate to reduce the\nperigee height to 5200 Kin. for a rapid re-entry and decay (in less than a day) of the debris in the\natmosphere.\nThe risk of such a strategy is the marginal increase in collision probability with the\nInternational Space Station due to debris transiting the 400 Km. altitude regime. However, as is evident\nfrom the figures above, the risk due to the current environment is very low - in fact the collision probability\nofa Intemational Space Station with a debris piece of >l cm. size is estimated to be I in 70 years. Hence,\ndespite the fact that an ORION system could "remove" - 100 debris objects per day, the increase in risk to\nthe International Space Station is minimal. Thus, the best strategy for ORION is the reduction of perigee\nheights of irradiated objects to below 200 Kin. However, according to Zeiders and Phipps, the penalty of\nthis strategy is a requirement of laser average power of 150 kW. (for debris of Type A) to 500 kW. (for\ndebris of Type D).\n2.3. The\n\n"Steady\n\nRain"\n\nStrategy\n\nThe major reason to look at an alternative strategy is the possible limitation on the power output\nof the laser. There is no extant laser that will meet the average and peak power requirements\nof the ORION\nsystem but lasers that can be scaled to meet the requirements\nessential to derive a strategy for debris removal that achieves\n\nmay be available.\nthe following:\n\nGiven such a system,\n\nit is\n\n!16\n\ni. The acquisition sensor should develop and use a search strategy that maximizes the probability of\ndetection of these debris and should preferentially\nhand these debris offto the laser for irradiation.\nThe laser should ensure\n\nthat the debris is irradiated\n\nadequately\n\nto reduce the perigee\n\nheight by 100\n\n- 200 Km. The laser system or the radar should then track the debris so as to assess the change in perigee\nheight to facilitate book-keeping\nand to ensure that the perigee has not been put into or below the\nInternational\nSpace Station altitude bin. If it has, then more tracking resources must be brought to bear to\nassess any risk to the International\nSpace Station until the perigee height decreases below 200 Km.\nA great advantage of this strategy is that the point of irradiation becomes the apogee of the orbit.\nFurther, because the inclination of the orbit is close to the critical inclination, the argument of perigee\nmoves very slowly. Thus, further apparitions of the same debris over the laser would be near the apogee of\nthe orbit which, according to Zeiders, is the preferred point of irradiation for maximal effect on reducing\nthe perigee\n\nfurther\n\nheight.\n\nOnce the perigee altitude of the debris piece has been reduced in steps to the 400 Km. bin., any\nirradiation should seek to lower the perigee to 200 Km. so that the debris can decay rapidly.\nThe implications\n\nof the "steady\n\nI. Recommendations\nThe removal of debris in one irradiation remains the best strategy for debris mitigation. However,\nno such laser of adequate power and operational capability is expected to exist in the near future. Hence, a\nstrategy for operations is recommended\nfor the ORION system that will enhance its effectiveness\nin its task\nof cleaning out the debris environment.\nThis strategy takes advantage of the debris density concentration\nin\nthe 800 - 1000 Km. altitude band and recommends\na staircase mode or "steady rain" technique of removing\ndebris.\n\n118\n\n6. Why Radars\n\nas transmitters.\n\nThis chapter\n\nanalyzes\n\nthe use of\n\n?\n\nThe advantages of microwave radars are the following:\nI. Radars are generally capable of all-weather day/night operation thus enabling the ORLON laser\nto work in cloudless day and night conditions.\n3. The mechanical/electronic\ndynamics of the radar permit stare-and-chase\nneeded\n\nfor ORION\n\nand also permit\n\nThe disadvantages\nof microwave\n1. The radars are high cost items\n2. Generally, the high sensitivity\ncates the search and acquisition\nput.\n3. Existing\nOn balance,\n6.3.\n\nThe Choice\n\ndiscrete\n\noperations\n\nas are\n\nof debris tracking.\n\nradars are:\nifa new system has to be procured (see Chapter 12).\nradars have a narrow instantaneous\nfield-of-view\nwhich compliprocess and requires creative techniques for enhancing through-\n\nradars are not optimally\nmicrowave\n\na high throughput\n\nlocated\n\nfor laser operations.\n\nradars are an attractive\n\noption for the ORLON system.\n\nof Radars\n\nMicrowave radars operate at a range of frequencies from VHF (150 MHz) to W-band (95 GHz) in\nfrequency regions. The debris sizes (1 - 10 cm.) that we are considering is a major driver in the\n\nchoice of frequency. Below L-band (-1300 MHz), the radar cross-section of debris smaller than 5 cm. is so\nsmall as to preclude effective detection.\nFurther, for a given small debris ( 1 - 5 cm.), the radar crosssection at > 10 GHz. frequency is -10 dB higher than at L-band or S-band (2 GHz.). Hence, the desirable\nrange of frequency\n\nof operation\n\nof a radar is X-band\n\n(I 0 GHz.) or higher.\n\nAt present, the high power tubes for X-band radars are easily available while for higher frequencies, such tubes are experimental,\nparticularly\nfor the high powers (-> 100 kW) required by this applica119\n\ntion. ence X-band istheideal\nH\na\nradar\noption\nwithaC-band\n(4GHz.) eing\nb\nanattractive\nalternative.\nHigher\nfrequencies\noftheorder fKu-band\no\n(16GHz) nd\na K-band\n(35GHz.) ay\nm beattractive\nalternatives\ninafewyears.\nThere\nareweather\nconsiderations\nweather\n\nhas negligible\n\nto be taken into account in radars too. Below C band, the\neffect on the radar. At X-band and above, moisture in the air and rain take an in-\n\ncreasing toll on the sensitivity of the system. For example, the sensitivity of an X-band radar could decrease by 3 dB. in heavy rain while the K- and W-band radars would suffer substantially\nlarger attenuation\nin humid atmospheres.\nHence it is preferable to operate a radar for the ORION system at lower frequencies.\nThe available radars and their parameters in the frequency ranges of interest are given in Table\n6.1. The HA VE\nSTARE radar can be upgraded to nearly Haystack \'s performance\nand would then be viable for the task.\n6.4. Operation\n\nof Haystack\n\n(or similar)\n\nRadar\n\nfor ORLON\n\nA concept of operations will be described in this section for a radar to act as the "debris finder"\nfor the ORION laser. As part of the concept, the requirements/capability\nto perform all the functions tabulated in 6.1 will be stated.\n\n6.4.1. Autonomous\n\nDetection\n\nof Debris\n121\n\nIt is\n\nessential\n\nfor the radar to have adequate time to acquire, track, discriminate\n\nand handover\n\nthe\n\ntarget to the laser. The discrimination task will take several minutes to complete and hence it is essential for\nthe radar to acquire the debris early in its apparition. Hence, the optimum strategy is for the radar to point\nat -300 elevation and conduct a small scan. The choice of azimuth is dictated by the location of the radar\nand the inclinations of the orbits that are of prime interest. Since most of the debris are in high inclination\norbits, a radar on or near the equator could point due north or south at 300 elevation for detection. However, Haystack is located at 42.60 north latitude and hence pointing due south is recommended\nproves the inclination coverage significantly (see Appendix 6.1).\n\nas it im-\n\nThe Haystack radar (or an upgraded HAVE STARE radar) has a very small beamwidth\n(instantaneous field-of-view)\nof the order of 0.05 \xc2\xb0. Long experience with Haystack has established that in\na stare mode pointing straight up, the radar detects, using a 1 ms. pulse, an average of 6 debris targets/hour\n(see Chapter 2) between the altitudes of 500 Km. and 1500 IOn. At an elevation of 30 \xc2\xb0, the radar loses -9.5\ndB in sensitivity due to the increase in range for the same altitude range. However, using a 5 ms. pulse\nmode, the radar can regain 7 dB in sensitivity. Additionally, the debris targets transit through the beam at a\nslower angular rate (see appendix 6.1) thus allowing multi-pulse summation to retrieve the remaining sensitivity "loss". Hence, we expect that the rate of detection would be of the order of 6 targets/hour in this\nmode. However, this has to be established by experiment in Phase 2. Unfortunately, half of these targets\nwill be setting. Out of the three left, only one might come into the field-of-view\nof the laser. Therefore,\nmethods have to be sought to enhance the rate of detection.\nDetection\nfor such a scan:\n\nstatistics can be enhanced\n\nby conducting\n\na scan with the radar.\n\nThere are three modes\n\n1. A mechanical "bow-tie" scan of-20 beamwidths which can be essentially "leakproof"\nand will\ncover a 1\xc2\xb0 swath in azimuth. Since there is no requirement for the scan to be leak-proof, a larger\nscan can be employed if it is consistent with antenna dynamics.\n2. An electronic scan that can be imposed on the beam by building a phased-array "lens" into the\nhigh power beam path between the feed and the Cassegrainian subreflector. Such a capability was\ndesigned for the HAVE STARE but was never built. It is fairly expensive and also reduces the\nsensitivity by about 2 dB.\n3. An electronic scan that is generated by redesigning the high power feed as a small phased array.\nThis has the advantage of avoiding the sensitivity loss but is still a complex upgrade.\nIt is our recommendation\n\nthat the mechanical\n\nscan be tested in Phase 2. The other techniques\n\nare expen-\n\nsive (several million) and complex and shouM be resorted to only if the mechanical scan cannot satisfy the\nappetite of the laser. The gain in detection statistics to be expected increases at least linearly with the scan\nwidth and should be verified in Phase 2.\n6.4.2.\n\nPrecision\n\nTracking\n\nof Debris\n\nOnce a debris target is detected the radar has to initiate tracking in what is essentially\nfrom a \'stare" mode to a "chase" mode. This is a classic capability of most radars for detecting\njects with large radar cross-section. However, the chase operation for a debris with small RCS\nof interest to ORLON system is more challenging because the S/N ratio in the monopulse angle\nnot large. It is the signal in these channels over several pulses that enable the radar to determine\ntion and rate of movement and initiate a chase.\nThe HAX radar, collocated\n\nwith the Haystack\n\nradar has recently\n\ndeveloped\n\na transition\nspace obof the types\nchannels is\nthe direc-\n\na "stare-and-chase"\n\ncapability for debris targets. Since both Haystack and HAX share the same control system, the "stare-andchase" algorithms can be transitioned\nto Haystack with small modifications.\nWhile the Haystack radar does\nnot support the high angular rates of the HAX (see Table 6.1), we believe that it is still capable\n"stare-and-chase"\nmode. Again, this is amenable to test in Phase 2.\n122\n\nof the\n\nThe Haystack radar can track in four dimensions\ncurrent\n\nprecision\n\nin these dimensions\nElevation\nAzimuth\n\n- azimuth,\n\nelevation,\n\n10- 35 prad.\n((10 - 35 )/cos(elevation))\n\nrange and range\n\nrate. The\n\nprad.\n\nis:\n\nRange\n0.25 - 2 meters\nRange Rate\nI - 10 millimeters/second.\nin the metric data from the Haystack radar are of the same order as the precision.\n\nBias uncertainties\nAccurate\n\ntracking\n\nof the debris is required\n\nto ensure that the acquisition\n\nwindow\n\nfor the laser is\n\nnot large. The handover volume is dominated by the angle uncertainty\nand, at worst, is of the order of 35\nprad which translates to 35 meters at 1000 Km. This is certainly acceptable to the acquisition mode of the\nlaser, lf a smaller handover volume is required, near-real-time\nprocessing\nof the metric data is required\nwith a Kalman-type filter, along with better calibration techniques. These are available and amenable to\ntesting in Phase 2.\n6.4.3. Discrimination\nThis is probably\n\nthe most time-consuming\n\nand complex\n\ntask for the radar. The requirements\n\nare as\n\nfollows:\n1. Verify that the debris is in an ascending pass.\n2. Ascertain the catalog status of the debris in track.\n3. Ensure that the estimated size and, if required, dynamics\n\nof the debris are within the capability\n\nof the laser.\n4. Measure periodicities\nin the signature.\n5. Check whether the debris will transit the laser field-of-view\n\nfor the time interval\n\nlaser system to successfully irradiate it.\n6. Guarantee that no other resident space object, and in particular,\n\nno payloads\n\nrequired\n\nby the\n\nwill be illumi-\n\nnated by the laser inadvertently\nduring the engagement.\n7. Guarantee that no airplane intercepts the laser beam during the engagement.\n6.4.3.1.\n\nCorrelation\n\nwith the Catalog\n\nThe monopulse data recorded during the transit of the debris through the beam is adequate to discern whether the target is in an ascending pass. If not, the search can be resumed. As soon as - 30 seconds\nof metric data (or -5 observations)\nare taken, an initial orbit can be estimated and checked to see if the\ndebris will be within the field-of-view\nof the laser for the required time interval during this apparition. If\nnot, the radar can return to its search scan. A correlation\n\nwith the catalog should be done next. The data\n\nquality is adequate to yield a good estimate of the orbit of the debris which can be checked against all the\nRSOs in the catalog. Measure the plasma "flash" created by the laser-particle interaction.\n2. Measure the "instantaneous"\nDoppler change of the target as a result of the interaction.\n3. A visible\nwith the laser, can measure this effect. It is unknown whether\n\nthere will be an enhancement of the radar cross-section as a result of the plasma though experience with\nobserving large transtage thrusts indicates otherwise. The flash will clearly indicate that the target has been\nhit. It is unknown whether the plasma will be quenched rapidly enough such that the interaction due to\neach pulse can be monitored.\nThe Doppler of a target can be measured very precisely by a microwave or laser radar using techniques of Fourier Transforms.\nAlso, depending on the accuracy of the track, Doppler can be inferred from\nrange measurements. In either case, if the target is monitored while being irradiated by the laser, the departure of the measured Doppler from prediction based on the pre-radiation\norbit is a clear and rapid indicator of laser effects. This technique is routinely applied at Lincoln radars for monitoring orbital maneuvers. However, it must be remembered that if the radar tracks the debris along with the laser, it cannot provide an advance guard to protect against inadvertent radiation of RSOs.\nContinued tracking of the debris post-radiation\nwill yield an estimate of the periodicity of the signature. This is very likely to have changed as a result of the laser-debris interaction and can both confirm\nthe interaction and, perhaps, provide a quick but poor estimate of the moment of inertia of the debris. Located\nin Tyngsboro, Massachusetts, this radar is part of the Lincoln Space Surveillance Complex and operates at\n10 GHz. with a 35 meter antenna. Its advantage is its high sensitivity. Its disadvantage is the relatively\nnorthern location which will preclude its effective tracking of debris in low inclinations.\nHaystack, HAX and Millstone Hill radars are part of the Lincoln Space Surveillance Complex\nlocated at --42.6 \xc2\xb0 North latitude. Invoking such a major element to provide acquisition and tracking looked difficult so a\npassive system was also examined in some detail.\n7.4\n\nPassive\n\nOptical\n\nAcquisition\n\nPassive acquisition and tracking of (large) space objects in low altitude orbits can be accomplished\nwhen the objects are in terminator illumination around sunrise and sunset. Acquiring and tracking in the\nterminator mode means that the sky background is dark so that the dim target light doesn\'t have to compete\nwith sunlight scattered by the atmosphere.\nSuch acquisition has been routinely accomplished\nfor large\nobjects -- typically satellites or spacecraft -- and less routinely for small objects. The stressing target in\nthe ORION group of targets is quite dim corresponding\nto a star of visual magnitude around 18 or 19. This\nis not routine.\n\nThe anticipated operation of an autonomous passive optical acquisition system is "stare and chase."\nThe system will be pointed at a fixed position in the sky "staring" over its field of view with a fixed\nintegration time (frame rate). The dwell time of the array is set equal to the time it takes this\ntarget, at its range and zenith angle, to cross the pixel FOV represented on the abscissa. Since this target is\nthe dimmest in the set and at longest desired range, this figure represents the most stressing limit. The solid line represents the S/N ratio:\nelectrons to the total noise standard deviation (sigma) also in photo-electrons.\n\nthe ratio of signal photo-\n\nAs indicated, the maximum signal-to-noise\nratio is about 2 for this most-stressing\ncase and occurs\nat a pixel FOV of about 20-60 Ixrad (with dwell times of about 8-25 msec). It is recognized that operation\nat this signal-to-noise\nratio is marginal.\nIt represents a probability of detection of 0.7 and a false alarm\nprobability of 0.1. (Adjusting the threshold to increase the probability of detection would also increase the\nprobability of false alarm.) However, it is anticipated that a fairly simple multiple hit track initiation\nalgorithm could be used to process multiple detections which would increase the probability of detection\nwithout increasing the probability of false alarm. Furthermore,\nthe target chosen is extremely dim (at the\nrange chosen it corresponds to about a 19th magnitude star) and an increase in brightness by only 50%\nwould increase the probability of detection to about 0.99 with no increase in false alarm probability for\nsingle pulse detection.\nIn Figure 7.3 is shown the same plots for a small target at an altitude of 1000 km with a range of\n1700 km at a zenith angle of 60*. The peak S/N ratio remains at about 2 since, while the range decreases,\nthe angular rate increases and the dwell time decreases.\nFigures 7.4 and 7.5 show the effects of zenith angle and background.\nAs can be seen in Figure\n7.4, the effects of zenith angle from 0 \xc2\xb0 to 60 \xc2\xb0 are not large giving good flexibility in locating targets as\nearly as possible.\nIn Figure 7.5 is shown the effect of full moonlit night on background\nwhich drops the\nsignal-to-noise\nratio by about a factor of 2 at the maximum of the signal-to-noise\ncurve. This is significant\nbut not overwhelming;\nsomewhat brighter targets than the most stressing would still be detected and\ntracked.\n7.4.2\n\nAcquisition\n\nRates\n\nThe current Lincoln\n\nLaboratory\n\nCCD focal plane referred\n\nto above is a 2500 x 2000 pixel array\n\nwith a pixel size of 25 Inn. Using this size directly for a 40 larad pixel FOV would imply, for a 3.5\ntelescope, an f/number of 0.15 -- quite impractical optically.\nHowever, if 12x 12 sub-arrays of these\nwere binned into a super-pixel,\nit would be 300 lain on a side and for a 40 grad super-pixel FOV, the\nsystem would be about f/\'2 -- much more practical.\nThis binning can take place on the chip so that\nread-out noise for a super-pixel remains at 10 electrons/read.\nWith 12x12 pixels per super-pixel, the\n\nm\np ixels\noptical\nthe\nwhole\n\narray would have 200x167 super-pixels.\nThe array FOV becomes 8 mrad x 6.67 mrad which is about 50\ntimes that of Haystack. The acquisition rate will depend upon the shape of the FOV and the distribution\nof\norbit angles but it will be at least 8 times that of Haystack thus essentially equalling (perhaps exceeding)\nthe number of targets acquired by Haystack per day.\n7.5.\n\nOperation\n\nof an\n\noptical\n\nacquisition\n\nsystem\n\nfor\n\nORION\n\nA concept of operations will be described in this section for the canonical optical system defined\nearlier to act as the "debris f\'mder" for the ORION laser. time to acquire, track, discriminate\nand\nhandover the target to the laser. The discrimination\ntask will take several minutes to complete and hence it\nis essential for the optical system to acquire the debris early in its apparition.\nHence, the optimum strategy\nis for the optical system to stare at -30 \xc2\xb0- 450 elevation.\nSince the optical system has a FOV of --0.5 \xc2\xb0, a\nscan is not needed though a "step-stare" scan could be used. The stare will keep the stars invariant in the\nFOV. The choice of azimuth is dictated by the location of the optical system and the inclinations of the\norbits that are of prime interest. Since most of the debris are in high inclination orbits, a optical system on\nor near the equator could point due north or south at 30 o elevation for detection.\nIf however, the optical\nsystem is located in say New Mexico, at 32 o north latitude, pointing\nimproves the inclination coverage significantly\n(see Appendix 6.1).\n\ndue south is recommended\n\nas it\n\nThe optical system should detect an average of 40 - 60 debris targets/hour between the altitudes of\n500 Km. and 1500 Kin. However, this has to be established by experiment in Phase 2. Unfortunately,\nhalf\nof these targets will be setting. Out of those left, only 1/3 might come into the field-of-view\nof the laser.\nIf a higher detection rate is desired, the optical system could conduct a "step-stare" scan.\n7.5.2.\n\n_\'ansition\ndeveloped\n\nPrecision\n\nTracking\n\nof\n\nDebris\n\nOnce a debris target is detected the optical system has to initiate tracking in what is essentially\nfrom a \'stare" mode to a "chase" mode. Such stare and chase operation for debris has been\nand demonstrated\nAfter the transition\n\nat the Experimental\nto "chase,"\n\na\n\nTest System (ETS) in New Mexico.\n\nthe acquisition\n\nsystem will be continuously\n\ntracking\n\nthe debris\n\ntarget. In the acquisition mode, with super-pixels of the order of 100 Ixrad, the tracking accuracy can be\nexpected to be in the range of a fraction of the super-pixel size -- the fraction depending on the S/N.\nThe Pusher\n\nLaser will most likely require\n\na pointing\n\naccuracy\n\nin the region of 1 I.trad. This could\n\nbe accomplished\nby a separate optical tracker (passive or active) with appropriately\nsized detectors or focal\nplane arrays so that the overall field of view is sufficient to accept handover from the acquisition system\n(with its wacking jitter/accuracy)\nand sufficient resolution to achieve the required precision tracking\naccuracy.\nIndeed, with the focal plane array used in the acquisition system as described above, it may be\nquite feasible to reconfigure\nthe focal plane array while still in acquisition track to utilize the much smaller\npixels (non-super-pixels).\nThese might have a pixel field of view in the few larad region and would afford\nthe required precision tracking required..\n7.5.3.\n\nDiscrimination\n\nThis is probably the most time-consuming\n\nand complex\n\nrequirements are as follows:\n1. Verify that the debris is in an ascending pass.\n2. Ascertain the catalog status of the debris in track.\n3. Ensure that the estimated size and, if required, dynamics\n\ntask for the optical\n\nsystem.\n\nThe\n\nof the debris are within the capability\n\nof\n\nthe laser.\n4. Measure periodicities\nin the signature.\n5. Check whether the debris will transit the laser field of regard for the time interval required\nlaser system to successfully irradiate it.\n6. Check that the debris will be sun illuminated until handover is complete.\n7. Guarantee ttu2t no other resident space object, and in particular, no payloads\n\nby the\n\nwill be illuminated\n\nby the laser inadvertently during the engagement.\n8. Guarantee that no airplane intercepts the laser beam during the engagement.\n141\n\nThe f\'LrStix of these can readily be accomplished\ns\nperiod.\n7.5.3.1.\n\nCorrelation\n\nwith\n\nby the optical system during the acquisition\n\nthe\n\nand track\n\nCatalog\n\nThe data recorded during the transit of the debris through the FOV is adequate to discem whether\nthe target is in an ascending pass. If not, the search can be resumed. As soon as - 75 seconds of metric\ndata (or -15 observations) are taken, an initial orbit can be estimated and checked to see if the debris will be\nwithin the field-of-view\nof the laser for the required time interval during this apparition. If not, the optical\nsystem can re_urn to its search. A correlation with the catalog should be done next. The data quality is\nadequate to yield a good estimate of the orbital plane of the debris and of its position in space, both of\nwhich can be checked against all the RSOs in the catalog. This task should take no more than 5 seconds\nwith a modern work station and appropriate architecture of the software.\nIf it is a known large RSO, the\nsearch for debris can be resumed. If it is a cataloged piece of debris, a real-time decision needs to be made\nbased on the following:\n1. Is it of interest to the ORION\n\nsystem - depending\n\n2. Who nominally "owns" the cataloged\nthe "owners" to irradiate their debris?\nGiven a positive\n\nanswer\n\nto both questions,\n\n7.5.3.2.\n\nSize\n\nand\n\ndebris?\n\non strategy and size?\n\nDoes the ORION\n\nsystem have "permission"\n\nfrom\n\nthe next step can be taken.\nDynamics\n\nAs the tracking of the debris piece continues,\n\nthe optical\n\nsystem must estimate\n\nthe mean size and\n\nperhaps a variance. The size can be approximately estimated using an average photometric phase model for\ndebris. There is considerable doubt about the "average" value for albedo to be used which directly affects the\nestimate of size. It is recommended\nthat, during Phase 2, a large sample of debris be examined with\nparticular bias towards the denser population regions to assess an average albedo. Techniques for decoupling\nthe area and albedo have been developed _. The signature data must also be analyzed through the mechanics\nof algorithms like auto-correlation\nor Fourier transform to determine any periodicities.\nThe estimation of\nsize is significantly affected by periodicity in the signature and, further, debris are known to have\nperiodicities\nranging from -4). 1 sec. to >>30 see.\nThe value of estimated periodicity in the signature lies in the fact that it will be significantly\naffected by the impact of the laser energy and, hence, it can be used as an indicator of the success of the\nengagement.\nThe inferred size of the debris must be compared to a threshold set for the ORION system to decide\non the engagement.\nThe periodicity may prove useful for the same purpose.\n7.5.3.3.\n\ndamaging\n\nInadvertent\n\nIllumination\n\nof\n\nRSOs\n\nA major concern with the ORLON system is its potential for inadvertently\na payload in orbit. This concern is motivated by both treaty implications\n\nilluminating and\nand the cost of\n\n"pienaty_e".\nOnce the debris has passed the t-dters in the previous sections and deemed suitable for engagement\nby the ORION system, a detailed prediction needs to be made of the part of the trajectory that the laser\nwould illuminate.\nThis prediction has to be compared with the known position of the entire catalog of\npayloads to guarantee that inadvertent illumination does not occur. Unlike RSOs, aircraft do not follow predictable trajectories.\nis prudent to choose a site where major air traffic lanes can be avoided.\nsystem needs a real-time means of detection and avoidance of aircraft.\n\nIt\n\nBut, in any case, the ORION\n\nOften a local search radar is used to minitor aircraft in the area, to warn them off or to avoid\nillumination in their direction.\nSince the FAA is shutting down a significant part of their radar system due\nto reliance on GPS technology, such a system may be available to the ORION system "free".\nIn addition,\nit may be possible to include a wide FOV optical system operating\nadditional target avoidance capability.\n\nlocation\n\nThis is a major\nissue for\nand modes\nof operation.\n7.5.4.\n\nOptical\n\nSystem\n\nthe\n\n- Laser\n\nORION\n\nsystem.\n\nin advance\n\nIt\n\nwill\n\nof the main laser to provide\n\naffect\n\ndecisions\n\non\n\nsite\n\nHandover\n\nOnce a debris has passed all the l-dters listed above, it has to be handed off to the ORION laser for\nirradiation.\nThe process in concept is very simple as the precision tracking of an optical system is adequate\nto narrow the search volume for the laser. There are two types of handover.\nA real-time\n\nhandover\n\noccurs when the optical system and laser are collocated.\n\nIn this case, the\n\nonly issue is the mutual calibration of the laser and the optical system. This is not a major issue as\nsubstantial experience exists at MIT Lincoln Laboratory and other places. The optical system continues\ntrack the object until a successful handover has taken place.\nA non-real-\n\ntime handover\n\noccurs when the optical system and the laser are not collocated.\n\nto\n\nIn such\n\na case, the optical system will have to determine a precise orbit and transmit it in some form to the laser\nsystem. The accuracy of the prediction is an issue that is being studied by AF Phillips Laboratory.\nAgain,\nprecise pointing calibration of both systems is a solvable concern.\nConcerns pertaining to the handover for both real-time and non-real-time\n2 using the collocated and spatially dispersed set of MIT Lincoln installations.\n\ncan be addressed\n\nin Phase\n\n143\n\n7.5.5.\n\nAssessment\n\nA critical issue is the assessment\nthat need to be answered are:\n1. Did the\n2. Can the\n3. Can the\n4. What is\n\nof the effects of the laser irradiation\n\non the debris. The questions\n\ntarget interact with the laser energy?\nmass, area/mass ratio or some similar parameter for the debris be estimated?\ncharacteristics of the laser-debris interaction be measured or inferred?\nthe perigee bin of the target post-irradiation?\n\n5. Is there a threat to a manned\nThere are four methods\n\nasset as a result of the orbit change?\n\nthat can be used to perform\n\nthese assessment\n\ntasks:\n\n1. Measure the plasma "flash" created by the laser-particle\ninteraction.\n2. Measure the "instantaneous"\nDoppler change of the target as a result of the interaction.\n3. Measure the change in the periodicity of the signature.\n4. Compare the estimated orbits pre- and post-irradiation.\nThe plasma "flash" is expected to occur on every pulse of the laser that hits the target. A visible\nwavelength\noptical system, if collocated with the laser, can measure this effect. The flash will clearly\nindicate that the target has been hit. It is unknown whether the plasma will be quenched rapidly enough\nsuch that the interaction due to each pulse can be monitored.\nThe Doppler of a target can be measured very precisely by a coherent laser. Also, depending on the\naccuracy of the track, Doppler can be infened from range measurements.\nIn either case, if the target is\nmonitored while being irradiated by the laser, the departure of the measured Doppler from prediction based\non the lyre-radiation orbit is a clear and rapid indicator of laser effects. This technique is routinely applied at\nLincoln radar systems for monitoring orbital maneuvers.\n\nsignature.\n\nContinued tracking of the debris post-radiation will yield an estimate of the periodicity of the\nHowever, it is quite unlikely that the debris target will continue to be illuminated by the sun and\n\nprobably a laser or microwave radar system would have to provide the tracking. The periodicity is very\nlikely to have changed as a resdt of the laser-debris interaction and can both confirm the interaction and,\nperhaps, provide a quick but poor estimate of the moment of inertia of the debris. Further, the laser\ntracking data can be Im3cessed\norbit, can yield the following.\n\ninto an estimate\n\nof the orbit which, when compared\n\nwith the pre-radiation\n\n1. An estimate of the total velocity change imparted to the debris.\n2. The perigee bin into which the debris has been moved.\n3. An estimate of the mass of the debris if the intensity\nknown and the size of the debris is known.\n\nof the laser at the location of the debris is\n\nThe new orbit must be used immediately\nto assess whether the threat to a manned satellite has\nbeen increased.\nIf the new perigee height is lower than that of the manned asset, but is >200 Kin.,\ncataloging of the debris by further tracking is essential so as to provide adequate warning of close\napproaches.\n7.5.6.\n\nMiscellany\n\nBook-keeping of the debris merely refers to creating a histogram of the number of objects irradiated\nvs. the perigee bin in say 100 Kin. steps before and after. This is to ensure that the risk in lower altitudes\nis not unduly increased and applies only in the ease of the "steady rain strategy".\n144\n\nThethroughputthe optical\nof\n\nsystem is governed\n\nby the approximately\n\nplus the search time to find the debris. For a 5-m mirror looking at an\nR--0.3, 1.5-cm target, Dbd_/R -- 4 is the correct line to follow. Then, a 10-km spot size for a target at\n1500 km would return only 0.004 photons per kJ, that is, a 2.5-MJ pulse at the ground would be\nnecessary\nto get a 10-photon\nreturn!\nMore realistically,\nif we use a 6-m mirror, W = 30kJ and 4-km spot size in the longer-term\nsystem\n[for 2-year sky search time], then at R = 0.3, a debris target must be 4.5 cm in size to return 10\nphotons.\nCalculations\n\nsuch as this have\n\nbeen discouraging\n\nin the past.\n\nNow. imagine that we have two mirrors - closely colocated\n- a small one with high quality\ntransmit and receive, and a much larger, very low quality mirror surrounding\nthat to receive\nreturned photons, to take the place of the radar system.\n224\nExcellence in photonics at affordable rates\n\nto\nthe\n\nFigure 5: Average\nLaser Power Required\nto Receive\n10 photons per shot and Search Sky in 2\nyears shows the average power P = f2*W required to meet thes criteria simultaneously,\nvs. target\naltitude. That is, a 20-m diameter\ncollector\nseeing\n\na 1.5-cm\n\nIn other\n\nwords,\n\nparticle\nactive\n\nwith Bond\n\nalbedo\n\nlaser acquisition\n\nin\n\nR = 0.25.\nwill work\n\nwith a 20-m,\n\nlow quality\n\ncollecting\n\noptic.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The DRd_R cutoff if we were to use 3MW average power [a power level dictated by generation\nof\nuseful thrust at range with a CW laser] - and dwell long enough on each spot to generate the 10\nphotons - is 10 times smaller. However,\nfor CW acquisition,\nwe can see that more received photons\nare required compared\nto the pulsed illumination\ncase, since the target is moving and it is a streak that\nwe must create rather than just a dot on the focal plane array.\nThe spot size ds can be read off Figure 4, where it is seen that a search spot of order 4km is correct to\nget back 10 photons from a 30-10 pulse. Holographic\n\napproach:\n\nmake a very short oscillator\n\npulse,\n\nhave\n\nof executing\n\nTwo of these methods feature clever schemes to deliberately\ncompress\na longer pulse. Of these,\ninvolves the use of a holographic\ngrating pair to passively compress a so-called "chirped"\nlarge\nbandwidth\npulse of about l0 ns duration.\nThe second compression\nmethod uses the physics of\nStimulated\nBrillouin Scattering\n(SBS), Stimulated\nRaman Scattering\n(SRS) or both in a cascade\nprovide passive compression.\nThe third method is the "brute force"\namplifier of adequate bandwidth.\n\nPULSES\n\nand amplify\n\none\n\nto\n\nit in an\n\nGratings\n\nThe beauty of this technique\nis its nearly perfect energy convesion\nefficiency\n(in principle).\nHowever,\nthe difficulty\nis that these gratings are limited to about 1 meter in transverse\ndimension by current\ntechnology.\nInspection\nof the figure above will show that the chriped input pulse to the grating must\n\nNeodymium\nlaser\n10-ns pulse duration\nwith chirped output\n\n_\n-_ _\n._1--[_\n\n"bluer"\nfollows\nholo\n\n"redder"\nfollows\n\nportion bends\nlonger path\nportion\nshorter\n\nless,\n\nbends more,\n\nOutput\n\npath\n\nthen be not much longer than 3 ns, due to the finite\'speed\nof light. SBS/SRS\n\nCascade\n\ninput\n\nSBS\ne.g.\n\nmedium\nbenzene\n\nr\nThe advantage\nof this technique\nis its simplicity and totally passive operation in shortening\na long input\npulse, by as much as a factor of 10. The pulse reshaping\nthat results is due to strong satruation\nof the\ninput or pump wave by the leading oedge of the counterpropagating\noutput or "Stokes"\nwave. A similar\ndiagram describes\nthe behavior\nof a reverse SRS pulse compressor.\nSBS and SRS units can be used in\ncascade to obtain certain desirable effects.\nTotal compression\nratios of about 100 have been obtained, just about what we require in the ORION\nsystem (efficient conversion\nto 100 ps pulses which couple efficiently\nto the debris target, from cheap,\nrelatively\nlow energy 10 ns inputs).\nThe problem with this technique\nis: low efficiency.\nCompression\nratios of 100 go along\nefficiency\nwhich may be as low as 2%, unacceptable\nfor ORION. Such a low efficiency\nmake it less expensive\nto build and use a higher energy long pulse laser.\n3. Amplfiying\n\nI\n\na short oscillator\n\n100-psoscillator\n\nwith energy\nwould actually\n\npulse\n\nI\n\nne\xc2\xb0dymiumamplifier\n\n1\n\n_\n\n\xc2\xb0utput\n\nThis approach is deceptively\nsimple. It also depends, at the present time, on unattainable\ncombinations\nof laser parameters.\nThe problem is that the brightness\nintegral or "B-integral"\nwhich determines\nbeam\nbreakup due to nonlinear refraction\nin the glass host of a solid state laser system is the same integral,\napart from constants,\nthat determines\nenergy extraction\nefficiency.\nAnd, for 100-ps pulses, the result is\nthat efficient extraction\nis not yet possible for lOOps pulses if high beam quality is also required.\nThe consequence\nof all this is: At this time, the best choice is a 5 or 10 ns pulse\npulse is generated by a solid state laser system.
