Target\n\n1.\n\nEngagement\n\nPropagation and Engagement Conditions\n\npeak Intensity I Power at TBget, J/cm=/kJ\n\nDiffraction Requirements\n1000\n\n100\n\n10\n\n1\n\nI\n\nAlthough laser systems are\nnormally designed to match the\nbeam with the intended target,\nconditions for ORION are such\nthat even the largest reasonable\nbeam directors will tend to flood\nthe sky, spreading much of the\nenergy far beyond the relatively\nsmall debris particles. A laser\nbeam from a finite aperture\nspreads\nby\ndiffraction, the\nresulting peak intensity at range_\nR being given _by I = P/Z2R2\n=D2/4 S = P/(=d\'/4) where P is\n\n}\n\n1\n\n10\n\nSpot\nDWn_r,cm\n\n100/1\n\n10\n\n0.01\nIO0\n\nSllee_e\nAl_tu_Dlmt=_D_,m\n\nthe power reaching the t_/4\nplane, Z is the wavelength, ;c\nis the transmitting area, and d is the spot size. The Strehl ratio S accounts\nfor non-ideal propagation effects such as turbulence, and can be much less than unity in practice.\nSince most of the space debris of interest for mitigation by ORION is at altitudes from 800 to 1200 km, laser\nspot sizes of at least 20 cm (and perhaps much larger) can be expected even with short wavelength (1.06p\nfor Nd:Glass lasers) and with beam directors approaching the size of today\'s largest astronomical\ntelescopes. While these relatively large sizes exact a toll from the pusher lasers in view of the required\noutput power, it will be shown that they, together with the short wavelengths and large apertures that are\nneeded to achieve them, are nevertheless very costly in terms of both turbulence compensation and the\nassociated sub-microradian pointing accuracy, and a more complete optimization at the system level might\nyet recommend operation with even larger spot sizes.\n\n_c\n\nTurbulence and Adaptive Ootics\n\nAside from nonlinear propagation effects that can normally be avoided by reducing peak intensity levels, the\nmajor contributor to non-ideel beam spread and loss of Strehl ratio is atmospheric turbulence (see the figure\non the next page) which disrupts the beam by superimposing regions of varying refractive index (much like\nlenses.) Turbulence effects both telescope images and transmitted laser beams by reducing the coherence\nand scattering the light with short correlation scales (often reducing the effective aperture size from the full\none D to a much smaller Fried scale r0), whereas large scales tend to tilt the wavefront and steer the light.\nAdaptive optics attempt to counter these effects by measuring the distortion of a reference wavefront\ntraversing the path and introducing the conjugate (opposite) distortion in the primary wavefront. The source\nfor the reference wavefront can be, for example, a natural star, reflection from an object (like a target), or\nemission from a laser beacon projected from the ground. Turbulence of course varies both spatially and\ntemporally, so correlation between the two wavefronts will be lost ever increasingly large scales as any\ntransverse displacement of their axes is increased, an effect usually refewed to as anisoplanatism. The\ndegree of correction improves as the size d of the control zones is decreased, and d = ro generally\nrepresents a useful cost-effective compromise. Such correction can be achieved with state-of-the-art\nsegmented and/or deformable adaptive optics systems, but it should be realized that many thousands of\nelements will be required for the large apertures of interest for ORION - and costs for such systems\n255\n\nd0,m\nlO\n\nro, m\nC\n\nWavelngth )., p\n\n10\n\n100\n\n1\n\ne,, r.\'ad\n10\n\n10\n\n10o\n\n\'\n\nt\n/.\n\n1.O\n\n/Yi\n\n/_--_-;-_ ...........\n\n;Yi\n\nco , l, s/,\n\n\'f\n\n"/\n\nI/7\n\n"" i,\n\n-" Ji\n\ni ..........\n\n0.2S\n1\n\nr_\n\n0.75\n\n7.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\',\n\n1.0\n\no.=.\n\n" 7\n\n0.50\n\n/\n\n/\n\nOT "_.IIP"\n\n0.76\n\ni\n\ni\n\ndoS_\nI\n\n0.26\n\no\n\ncontinue to be at least about $1000 per channel (although $200-$250 per channel is recommended for use\nas an eventual goal.)\nSeveral typical distributions of atmospheric turbulence are\nshown at the right, and their effects on propagation are given in\nthe nomograrn. Turbulence always tends to be very high near\nthe surface due to the Earth\'s boundary layer (dominating the\ncontribution to ro), but locally-severe conditions can arise at\nhigher altitudes due to wind shear. High tropospheric winds in\nparticular often lead to pronounced turbulence at upper\naltitudes, and, although the resulting strength is such that they\ncontribute little to r0 itself, their distance from the aperture can\ncause important coherence losses between paths that diverge\nfrom the aperture.\n\nl\nol\n\ni\n\nThe latter effect is especially important for propagation to space\n\nI\n\n"_\'_--\n\n_oe\n\nto"\n\nto"\n\nl_\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 =\'_\nlo"\n\nT,,=.,==,,_c.,, ,,-\n\nbecause the "isoplanatic" angle over which coherence is Ixese_ed is usually much less than both the angle\nbetween the target and a sufficiently-bright\nnatural star beacon (typically spaced several hundred\nmicroradians) and the lead-ahead angle 2v/c cos0 required to account for the distance that the target moves\nin the round-trip transit time for light between the ground system and the target. The tilt anisoplanatism is no more than (0.00410)(13.5) = 0.055 i_rad in that case, so image\nmotion will be small compared to the diffraction size. Matters, however, can become considerably more\ncomplicated when correction must be made for a laser beam targeted against a rapidly-moving distant\nobject.\n\n_dJurn\n\n3.5\n\nThe high-altitude turbulence that most contributes to isoplanatic problems limits the\neffectiveness of the beacon as well because the solid angle from the beacon to the\naperture does not account for all of the turbulence along the full path, even with a\nsodium beacon which, at 92 km altitude, is the highest artificial one that has been\nidentified. This effect, commonly referred to as focus anisoplanatism, leads to the\nconcept of a "beacon coverage size" do that defines the largest aperture that can be\ncovered by a single beacon (a few meters at typical conditions.) Altematively, it\nalso can be used to determine the number of beacons N = (D/do)" that would be\nneeded for a large aperture, but each such beacon must be =tagged" to differentiate\nits wavefront from the others, and the multiple signals must then be "stitched"\nproperly to produce the proper final wavefront. Unfortunately, the ability to actually\nuse multiple beacons has lagged the theory, and there have been no truly\nsuccessful demonstrations at this time. Until then, although there appear to be no\nfundamental reasons why multiple beacons cannot be applied, the use of an\naperture larger than that which can be handled with a single beacon should be\nregarded for now as a very risky proposition.\n\nm\n\nThe status of a number of adaptive optics systems as of 1/94 is reviewed in the table on the next page.\n\n_rK]aoement\n\nProfile\n\nO,Jx=\nP_\n\nsystem, and serves to identify\n\n146\n\n219\n\n0\n\nRr_ _l_*\n\nAW_r*\n\nA\n\n..\n",\n:\ni\n,,\n\n93\n\nPu_\n\nThe engagement profile at the\nright shows some important\ncharactedstics of the ORLON\n\nn_, Q 1000 I=m\nNIIlude, seconds\n\n-,=\n""\n\n\xc2\xa2\n,\'\n"\n\n"\n\n,\n\n\'KaI_X\nm.mp-\n\n/. f\n\nJ\n\n/\n\nseveral\nas-yet\nunresolved\nissues.\nTimes and angular\nrates are specified for a 1000\nkm debris altitude, but the\nresults can easily be scaled to\nother altitudes.\n\nT,._\n_\n\n:_\n:=\'m_d\n,_\nI\n0._sc_>_=\n0_\n:\n_\n\n,\'\n,\'\n/\n\n,\'\n\n.."\n/ _\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n(.t,,_,,,_/_\nJ._=_._"\n\n@\'_0o0_ .J _\n.0_""\n\nThe target will typically be\n:: //,o"\ns,,m=_,=,_,-_=,,,,_v,_m\nengaged at about 60 _ from\n;:..-\'\n"J_"\nzenith (30 \xc2\xb0 above the horizon)\nbecause R" and atmospheric losses rapidly increase beyond there. Tyson, "The _us\n258\n\n13+\nlWNa\nLGS\n6\n\n2.2\n\n64 s_,_.\nS_-Harlm.\n\n2.Srn\nML W1bon\n\nJq_n\nOM "=V,Ohio\nSL,\nSVcFd\nO_.\n\nBt\n\n.05+\n\nSmmn_l \'F\nb\n\n3.5m ARC\n\nNAO-SUP%_._\'_.U\n\n6 _,,--_\n\n..2+\n\nof aa_,ucKxnicaladaptive optics systems". SPIE OE Reports (Jan 1994)\n\n13\n12W\nLGS\n50W\nLGS\n\nThe problem here is the need for "Kentucky Windage", the lead-ahead required to account for the distance\ntraveled by the target during the elapsed time for a signal from the target to arrive at the sensor, be\nprocessed for adaptive optics correction, and then for the laser beam to retum to the target. This is not a\nmeasured quantity, but, rather, must be calculated and established by dead reckoning over tens of\nmicroradians to an accuracy of the size of the laser spot (about two orders of magnitude smaller.) Unlike a\nduck hunter who is smart enough to use a shotgun instead of a rifle to shoot a fast-moving bird, the slowly\npulsed pusher laser wants to act like a rifle, and the relative size of the lead-ahead will make it very difficult\nfor a tracking system (active or passive) to provide the required accuracy by dead-reckoning regardless of\nthe certainty of orbital motion - especially when the laser "rifle" is being moved about by turbulence.\nThough typically only a few milliseconds_300tl_e\ndistances are far greater than target dimensions, and the leadahead angle 2V/c cose (42.4 p.rad at\nand 1000 km altitude) is generally so much larger than the\nisopianatic angle that the signal from the target will be useless as a beacon for the adaptive optics. A\ndedicated laser guidestar within the isoplanatic angle about the outgoing path (i.e., "leading" the target) will\ntherefore be essential for reducing the beam spread, but less direct, however, is the means for handling\nturbulence-induced tilt because the guidestar signal is itself steered by turbulence. The debris objects of\ninterest tend to be smaller than the beam size, and we have already shown for the case being considered\nthat the RMS tilt angle greatly exceeds the ideal diffraction angle (by a factor of over 20), so the probability\nof hitting the target will be extremely low if we do not accurately measure or predict the tilt.\nThe most attractive option for doing so appears to be to use the target itself as a reference for tilt, accurately\nbiasing the track and tilt data to account for the lead angle, and that should be possible because of the\nrelatively invariant nature of orbital flight. However, it should be realized that the combination of tilt\nanisopianatism and the need for highly accurate tracking data will require that the pusher beam and the\noptical signals from the target and the guideetar all be shared by the same aperture - a non-trivial hardware\nproblem -- because of the pronounced degradation that would otherwise result from strong low-altitude\nturbulence. Both the pusher beam and the target tracker must use adaptive optics to minimize spread, and\ntheir corrections will necessarily be different because\nT/UqGET\n\ntheir angular separation considerably exceeds the\nisoplanatic angle (let alone the fact that their wavelengths\nmay be different.) All of the wavefronts will essentially tilt\ntogether at a characteristic frequency VT/D ,= 10 Hz for\nthe given conditions, so the tracking data must be rapidly\nprocessed in view of the need for accuracy to a small\nfraction of the large overall excursion of the tilt The\nsmall time slices required for gathering the tracking data\nand the need to closely synchronize it with the pusher\nbeam strongly\nsuggest that the target\ntracking\nillumination be provided by either a CW or very-rapidlyrepped laser, probably operating at yet another\nwavelength to ease the difficulties of aperture-sharing. A\nsuggested functional block diagram of the system is\nshown at the right to illustrate the key components and\ninformation flow. The first splitter is the only high intensity\nshared component beyond the basic telescope/director.\nNeither the beacon nor the illuminator laser need, of\n\n-,\n\nmm\n\nm\nm\n\n_ _ ._ _\neco_ :croR\n\'\n_ _ .,_ ,_\no SENSOR\noco_\nI _ _\n\ne s_eoR\n\nI\n\ncourse, be co-located with the pusher/sensor system.\nShould extrapolation of the tracking data not provide sufficiently accuracy, it may be necessary to follow the\napproach of the duck hunter by broadening the beam considerably (i.e., to use a "shotgun" and to accept the\ngreatly decreased intensity) and/or to share the main aperture with a boresighted scanning laser (a CW or\nrapidly pulsed "machine gun\') that can be continually steered to maximize return from the target.\nRegardless of the approach, the complications involved in boresighting with a high energy pulsed taser\ncould prove to be so daunting that it might be worth considering the use of a far more powerful and rapidlysteered CW or highly-napped pusher laser to overcome both the lead-ahead and aperture tilt problems,\nprobably eliminating the need for a fine tracker at the same time.\n259\n\n2. LargeApertureTelescopes\nandBeamDirectom\nIt appeem berno_likelythata largebeamdirector ill be needed for ORION to produce an accapiab_\nto\nw\nspot size. Altho_h the op6cal telescopes described here are not beam directors per se, they are large\nenough to be directly applicable to ORION, and each in its own right is an exceptional Instrument that\ndisplays the design philosophy and coet_\nthat will be required for orbital debris removal with\nground-besed lien=.\n3.67M AEOS and 3.5M Sterllre T__\n\nThe 3.67 meter AEOS system shown above is presently under construction in Hawaii, and has extremely\nexpensive site-related costs that do not meet our objectives, but its functionally-similar predecessor, the 3.5\nmeter telescope at Starfire Optical Range in Albuquerque NM, does so admirably. The 27M$ total cost of\nthe latter included 10.5M$ for the Corttmves USA optical mount and 7M$ for the primary mirror from the\nUniversity of Arizona\'s Steward Observatory Mirror Laboratory. The very moderate telescope cost is due in\nlarge part to the use of a 4500 lb. monolithic primary mirror that broke from tradition by using a thin spuncast borosilicate facsplate with computer-controlled actuators to compensate for deformations of the\nhoneycomb sandwich base. Other unique features include a complete closed-cycle water heater/chiller\nsystem for precise themml control and a protective enclosure composed of three 9 ft. high, 70 ft. diameter\ncylinders that collapse around the telescope to provide complete ventilation and an unobstructed view for\nhigh-speed satellite tracking. Both systems are designed to monitor a variety of space objects, and their\nslew rates (18.3 deg/sec in azimuth and 4.75 deg/sec in elevation) are sufficient to track even very lowaltitude targets. They will eventually incorporate deformable adaptive optics at the end of the F/200 Coude\npaths to provide the desired resolution, but delivery of the 3.55M$ 200W sodium guide star from LLNL for\nSOR has been delayed by AF budget reallocation. The systems are specifically designed for imaging, and,\nas a minimum, the present silver-coated optics throughout the Coude path would probably have to be\nreplaced for high-power operation. A 12 in. 5 mrad full FOV scope with an intensified camera is available\nfor acquisition. The SOR site is located near the end of the runway of the Albuquerque IntemaUonel Airport,\nand FAA approval is required for individual shots.\n\n260\n\n10M Keck Telescopes\n\n_0=_..=__\n\n_o_=m_ow=\n\n........................ \xe2\x80\xa2\n__\n\n_.,..d_\n\n....\n\n__\n\nKeck I1: O_\n\nP,rimery\nmppo_\n\n_\n\nDrive & oonO\'ol\n\notx.m_o_deVo\nSUl_\n\nflldmes\n\nM=npolr\nKed\xc2\xa2 I"\n__\n\n_\n\n--\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n21 MS\n4\n..\n\n2\n\nS\n13\n\n_M=\n93M$\n\nDome\n\n10\n\n......\n\nEventually expected to operate as an interferorneter, the two 10 meter Keck astronomical telescopes on\nMauna Kea in Hawaii are the largest in the world, and they again broke with conventional design tradition by\neach employing an array of 36 hexagonal mirror segments, each in turn actively supported on three\nwhiffletrees with computer-controlled actuators to control distortion. The required overall parabolic figure\nwas obtained by precisely pre-stressing each of the mirror blanks, polishing them to a sphedcal curvature,\nand then allowing them to relax to the final shape. The overall weight of the Keck telescope is 596,000 Ibs,\nabout half that of the 5 meter Hale telescope, and about three times that of the Starfire telescope (186,000\nIbs.) Subsystem costs are shown in the figure, and it is quite significant that they are only about three times\nthose of the Starfire telescope. A 349-element deformable mirror from XJnetics will be available soon to\nprovide infrared turbulence correction (note that d = DI_/N = 54 cm is much larger than typical values of r0 in\nthe visible.) Although designed exclusively for astronomical observations, it is interesting to note that their\nmaximum design slew rate of 3 deg/sec ("anywhere to anywhere in under 2 minutes\') would be sufficient for\nthe needs of ORION.\n\nHiqh-Pow_" Beam Director\nNo high-power beam directors of ORION size exist in the Wastem world. The telescopes described above\nare designed for imaging, not for beam directing, and their optical designs attest to that fact. The large\nprimary mirrors themselves do not pose a sedous problem because the flux levels on them tend to be quite\nlow (e.g., 10 MW on a 10 meter mirror corresponds to only 0.13 Wlcm 2 - one solar flux - at 1% absorption),\nbut the smaller mirrors in the Coude path are typically subjected to 100 times or more intensity, and that will\ngenerally require more damage-resistant coatings and actively-cooled substrates.\nThe most vulnerable\nelements generally tend to be those associated with the adaptive optics system, e.g. the high-power beam\nsplitter for the wavefront sensor, and the active mirror itself (if it is in fact situated in the Coude path.)\nNonlinear beam propagation becomes a major issue as well within a telescope pdmadly because of the high\nintensities in the Coude path, and a high power beam director will usually employ beam conditioning using a\nsealed optical system with a low absorbing flowing gas, and that in turn will require transmitting windows to\nisolate the gas from the surroundings.\nThese _ould represent rather extensive changes of the existing systems (which, of course, are dedicated to\napplications that don\'t need those mods), and a concept that might be suitable for a large beam director to\nmeet the requirements of the ORION mission is the coelostat shown on the next page. This design was\nspecifically configured to use the PAMELA segmented primary mirror concept which permits r0-sized\nadaptive optic elements instead of ones reduced by the telescope magnification ratio to a few millimeters,\nand places them on a rigid base to minimize control-structure interactions and to allow active cooling if\n201\n\nr_e_,\n\nThe r_d _\n\n_\n\n_ov_\n\nst_lHy, _k_s\n\n_ady _\n\nth_ho_\n\nfor U_r_\n\nand\n\neleotro-mechwlical control, and enables the use of an active secondary for additional correction. The\nsealed rotating housing with a verticelly-mounted window protects all of the high-value components and\nprovides a controlled environment for the high intef1_ty\nlaser path. The most argumentative (dements of the\ndesign are the two massive 450 mirrors, but they execute rather sdmple motion, and can be of very\nlightweight design with active figure correction via the adaptive optics.\n\nCoat\nThe optics represent the moa fundamental unit of any telescope or beam director, and scaling philosophy in\nthe business places a premium on their total mass by assuming that other system and subsystem weights\nand costs are ell linearly proportional to it for s/re,far systems. Such scaling has little firm basis in\nengineering or science, but, rather, it is based on good design practice wherein improvements in one\ncomponent tend to be mirrored in others because of both competition and cost-effectiveness (because\nrelative Improvements in a minor component would better be made in a major one.). An accepted rule with\nastronomical telescopes is, in fact, that the cost of the total telescope and mount is about twice that of the\noptics, and the total facility is about twice the telescope. Both the Keck and the Starfire systems fit this\nguideline quite well with regard to cost, but the agreement may be somewhat fortuitous in view of the\ndisparities in telescope mass and mirror cost. The high slew rates of the Starfire telescope resulted in a\nmuch heavier telescope relative to the primary\n10\'\nmirror, but its lightweight mirror was relatively\nexpensive, it is thought, due to recovery of coats\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nK_R\nfor spin casting development.\nNot to be\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nCalven.on_ So_\ne=\ne\'"\ndiscounted, too, is the fact that another major\nI0\'\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nSona._\nfactor in any such system is the cost of high-tech\nOil\n..\nsupport, and Starflm\'s dedicated Air Force\nC.orWou_\n\xe2\x80\xa2 ,a"Si_rare/\npersonnel and Keck\'s university support probably\n10\'\nhad major impact on the very moderate overall\ncosts.\nThe problems associated with simple power-law\nscaling am shown at the right where mass is\nplotted for a large number of mirrors of various\ntypes of design. The Keck and Starflm minors\nthemselves actually follow conventional D2.e\nscaling, but they represent lower extremes of\nwidely scattered data, the dispersion of which can\nproduce huge numedcel differences at such large\nsizes.\nThose differences represent a major\n\nlo\'\n\n10\n\n1\nI0\nMi_r _,\n\n262\n\nM\n\nsystem technology and design ddver because high values can simply not be afforded, and paradigm shifts\nhave to be made. In the interest of cost realism. As a result, the overall cost of the Keck scales almost\nlinearly with D from Starfire, and a major change like the coelostat concept might perhaps be the next step\nthat would have to be taken with radical redesign for a much larger telescope.\nEconomic realism dictates that means be found to continue such a scaling trend.\n\n3.\n\nHigh-Energy Lasers\n\nThe choice of available lasers for the ORION mission is really quite limited because of the need to produce\na beam of high energy pulses at a high duty cycle on a regular basis for long periods of time and to\npropagate that energy through the atmosphere to a very small spot at long ranges in space.\nThe CO2 laser was the first to operate in a very high power mode, and its lower power versions today set the\nstandard for high reliability in the industrial world, but the 10 - 11rp wavelength is prevented by diffraction\nfrom producing the target energy density needed for ORION.\nAnother system that has attracted\nconsiderable commercial interest, partly because of its ability to actually break the chemical bonds of the\nmaterial that it interacts with, is the excimer laser which operates at the other end of the wavelength\nspectrum in the ultraviolet and short IJV; the potentially high single-pulse energy and small spot size has\nalso caused considerable interest for use as a space-based laser weapon, 10ut he available parameter range\nt\nof pulse energy, pulse length, and rep rate don\'t mate wall with the ORION requirements, and the short\nwavelengths greatly complicate the use of adaptive optics for turbulence correction. Similar arguments\npertain to most of the other high energy laser systems that have been developed for military and/or\nindustrial use (e.g., iodine @ 1.3rp, HF/DF @ 2.7 - 3.8rp, and CO @ 4.8 - 6rp), the result being that the only\nremaining viable candidates appear to be the neodymium glass and free electron lasers.\nSolid state neodymium glass lasers are widely used for inertial confinement fusion programs because of\ntheir ability to produce high energy in relatively short pulses at short wavelength - 1.06rp, but readily\ndoubled and tripled for operation in the visible. Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory has led this\ncountry in the development of such lasers through the massive Shiva, Nova, and soon-to-be NIF (National\nIgnition Facility) systems, and their currently-operating Beamlet laser with its 20 kJ, tens of nanosecond\npulses may be uniquely suited to the ORION mission if the system can be cooled for operation in a\nrepetitively pulsed mode. A major benefit of the concentrated R&D that has gone into those systems is the\navailability of specialized hardware for such functions as frequency doubling, beam isolation, and cleanup, a\nnotable example of the latter being the SBS (stimulated Bdllouin scattering) mirror that has been used at\nLLNL to generate a phase conjugated beam for real-time cancellation of thermal distortions. Caution is\nsimply advised that these lasers are very expensive, and their present location in the heart of California\'s\nheavily-populated Uvermore Valley is less than ideal from every viewpoint for ORLON. Nevertheless, LLNL\nhas performed ground-to-space propagation from there, and they have - or could soon have - the\ncapabilities on site to vividly demonstrate the debris clearing concept.\nThe free electron laser remains a candidate for ORION because of the advantages offered by its wide\noperational parameter space (including wavelength), potentially high efficiency with electron beam recovery,\nand excellent beam quality, but it also represents one of the most dramatic failures of the DoD high energy\nlaser program in terms of dollars spent per kilowatt output. It has, however, been pursued far mere\nsuccessfully in the former Soviet Union, and it and other systems developed there might prove more\nsuitable for orbital debris removal than would their US counterparts if certain biases by our government and\nits industrial partners can be overcome. The most serious technical question involving the use of an FEL for\ndebris clearing is its ability to actually produce the desired target interaction: for example, a 200 kW FEL\n(e.g., the Microtron RF system proposed for near-term delivery to the U.S. by the Budker Institute in\nNovosibirsk) operates at about 20 MHz with about 20 psec pulses, and, regardless of the details of the\nmacropulse format, this corresponds to no more than tens of millijoules of energy per pulse whereas target\ncoupling requires at least tens of joules at that pulse duration.\n263\n\nThebenefits a well-corrected\nof\nlargeaperture\nbeamdirectororORLON\nf\ncannot eunderstatedecause\nb\nb\nof\ntheprofound\neffectof the resulting small spot size on the laser system itself. A 10 cm spot on a target at a\nrange of 2500 km, about the most that would be necessary to deorbit the largest concentration of orbital\ndebris, would require an effective aperture size DVS of 32 meters at l rp wavelength. This is ciearly far\nbeyond even the largest optical astronomical telescopes being considered today and it would require a\npointing accuracy of at least 30 nanoradians, but such sizes are relatively routine with radio telescopes, the\nsegmented optics technology exists today to populate such structures for operation at optical frequencies,\nand the pointing accuracies are similar to those pursued for the Strategic Defense Initiative. Instead of the\ntens of Idlojoules/pulse that might be required with a more conventional telescope, the desired effect could\nbe obtained with less than 1 kJ pulses from a 50 nsec laser, and this corresponds to the pulse parameters of\nthe 20 MW induction FEL (I kJ pulses @ 20 kHz) that was the eady baseline for the SELENE system and for\nwhich the solid-state accelerator modules are already in operation at Science Research Laboratory in\nSomerville MA. Note that the 20 MW figure is for compadson only, and an operational ORION system\nusing either an FEL or a glass laser could operate at far smaller PRF and power level with those kJ pulses.\nFor near-term use, that same beam director operating against a target at 500 km would produce only a 2.5\ncm spot, but the same impulse could be obtained with only a few tens of joules par pulse.\n\n4.\n\nSpace Debris Removal - Orbital Considerations\n\nSpace debris can be deorbited by using a ground-based laser to selectively apply a velocity increment to\nincrease ellipticlty and/or to decrease energy. The reduced perigee altitude in either case will then result in\nincreased drag and more rapid destructive re-entry. The orbitallresntry aspects of the problem are fully\ntreated here, emphasizing physical understanding of the behavior rather than actual numerics. The wave forms across the exposed surface of the material, so the\nresulting force is normal to that surface. The time-integrated force from a single pulse is an impulse that in\nturn translates into a velocity increment, and the gross result for a train of pulses is a series of such Av\'s that\nmodify the orbit such that the integrated drag is increased and the particle more quickly reenters. To put\nthis in perspective, Av scales as C__E/M, so a particle with mass M of I gram receives a Av of only 0.1 mps\nfrom 1 joule of incident energy E at a coupling coefficient Cm of 10 dynes/watt; since typical orbital velocity\nin LEO is about 7900 mps, many such pulses will therefore be required to cause a significant change in the\norbit. Although eccentricity and perigee altitude (the two orbital parameters most affecting lifetime) may be\nsignificantly affected during the exposure time, the local velocity and radial distance will change little during\nthat period, so it is within the spirit of this analysis to treat the total impulse as purely incremental and to\nleave higher order effects to computer analysts.\nSince each laser pulse effectively produces an incremental velocity which does not affect the local radial\ncoordinate r but does change the other properties of the appropriate ellipse through that point, it is useful to\ndescribe the orbit in terms of its local velocity components Vr and ve, i.e.\nVr = IEH c sine, rye = H, H2/p.r= 1 + s cose.\n267\n\nThen,\n\nxy\ntane=\n\n_.-_.,\n\nx\'\ne=-_/(xa. 1)2+xay _, _=\n\n+---_\'\n1\n\nx= vo\'_/_\' Y=V,\'_/_\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nwhere _/pJr, the local circular velocity, is about 7900 mps in low Earth orbit as noted above. ASSTRACT(Maxknum2OOword$)\nAbout\ni00,000\npieces\nof\nI- to 10-cm\ndebris\nin low-Earth\norbit\nare\ntoo\nsmall\nto track\nreliably\nbut\nlarge\nenough\nto cripple\nor destroy\nspacecraft.\nThe\nORION\nteam\nstudied\nthe\nfeasibility\nof\nremoving\nthe debris\nwith\nground-based\nlaser\nimpulses.\nPhotoablation\nexperiments\nwere\nsurveyed\nand\napplied\nto likely\ndebris\nmaterials.\nLaser\nintensities\nneeded\nfor debris\norbit\nmodification\ncall\nfor pulses\non\nthe order\nof 10kJ\nor continuous\nwave\nlasers\non the\norder\nof\n1 MW.\nAdaptive\noptics\nare\nnecessary\nto correct\nfor\natmospheric\ndegradation\n\nturbulence.\nWavelength\nand\ndue\nto nonlinear\natmospheric\n\npulse\nduration\nprocesses.\n\nwindows\nwere\nDebris\ncan\nbe\n\nfound\ndetected\n\nthat\nlimit\nbeam\nand\nlocated\n\nto within\nabout\n10 microrads\nwith\nexisting\nradar\nand\npassive\noptical\ntechnology.\nFine\ntargeting\nwould\nbe accomplished\nwith\nlaser\nillumination,\nwhich\nmight\nalso\nbe used\nfor\ndetection.\nBistatic\ndetection\nwith\nconT, unications\nsatellites\nmay\nalso\nbe possible.\nWe\nrecommend\nthat\nexisting\ntechnology\nbe used\nto demonstrate\nthe\nconcept\nat a loss\nof about\n$20 million.\nWe calculate\nthat\nan installation\nto clear\naltitudes\nup to 800\nkm of\nI- to 10-cm\ndebris\nover\n2 years\nof operation\nwould\ncost\nabout\n$80 million.\nClearing\naltitudes\nup to 1,500\nkm would\ntake\nabout\n3 years\nand\ncost\nabout\n$160\nmillion.\n\n14.
